Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority are essential properties of things (e.g., father is inherently before son).
Correction: The passage shows that the substances themselves are neutral; priority comes from time.

Checkpoint
Why can the father and son be considered prior and posterior only in a conventional sense?
A) Because their essences necessitate priority.
B) Because priority and posteriority are not essential to their substances but come from time.
C) Because they are not real substances.
D) Because time is nothing.

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority are not essential to substances but are conventional, arising from temporal position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a relationship where you consider yourself 'prior' or 'superior' to someone else—perhaps due to age, status, or achievement. ?

Can you truly accept that your essence is neutral to such comparisons? Write down one specific way you will stop measuring yourself by temporal priority and instead focus on your intrinsic value.

Identify a specific area in your life (work, family, spirituality) where you strive to be 'first' and ask yourself: am I doing this because it is essential, or because of social convention? What would you do differently if you believed your essence is already complete regardless of temporal order?

Time and Duration in Islamic Theology
Gloss explains that time is an imaginary extension from motion, and without motion it is called dahr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between time (zaman) and duration (dahr) in Islamic theology and how they relate to motion.

Core Idea
Time is an imaginary extension derived from motion; without motion it is called dahr.

In this lesson, we explore a subtle distinction made by theologians: time is not a real entity but an imaginary extension that arises when we consider motion. When motion is not considered, the same underlying substrate is called dahr (eternity). This passage from the commentary clarifies these terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Get a feel for the commentator's language.
Focus on the words for time and duration.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms zaman, dahr, harakah, and imtidad wahmi in the Arabic text.
These are central to the discussion.
3
Understand the clarification about motion
Notice that the commentator says motion is not literal for the divine. This prevents misunderstanding.
The phrase 'حركة الواجب محال' is crucial.
4
Grasp the definition of time
Time is an imaginary extension that arises from the persistence of motion.
It is not a real entity but a mental construct.
5
Distinguish zaman from dahr
If motion is considered, it is zaman; if not, it is dahr (or jawhar according to one view).
This distinction is the lesson's core.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ووجدت إلخ) عطف تفسيري للجملة السابقة يعني : ليس المراد بحصول الحركة فيه بحركة فإنَّ حركة الواجب محال ولذا لم يقل ثم إِنَّ تحرك بل المراد أن يوجد لأجزاء الحركة نسبة إليه باستمراره في جميع الأجزاء حصل منه امتداد وهمي يسمى بالزمان على نحو ما قالوا الآن السيال؛ أنه يفعل باستمراره وعدم استقراره الزمان بمعنى الأمر الممتد في دهـراً. قوله : ( وإن لم توجد إلخ) أي وإن لم تعتبر نسبة الحركة تسمى قوله : ( وإن لم توجد الحركة إلخ قيل مرادهم أنه إن لوحظ وقوع الحركة فيه يسمى الزمان، وإن لم يلاحظ يسمى جوهراً سواء وقعت بالفعل أم لا
His saying: 'and there occurs [motion] in it' etc. is an explanatory conjunction of the preceding sentence, meaning: the intended meaning by 'occurrence of motion in it' is not that it moves, for motion of the Necessary is impossible; therefore he did not say 'then it moves'. Rather, the meaning is that for the parts of motion there is a relation to it (the substance) by its persistence through all parts, and from this arises an imaginary extension called time, in the manner of what they say about the flow of the fluid: that by its persistence and instability it produces time in the sense of an extended thing. And his saying: 'and if motion does not occur in it' etc.: that is, if the relation of motion is not considered, it is called dahr. And his saying: 'and if motion does not occur in it' etc. It is said: their intention is that if the occurrence of motion in it is considered, it is called time; if not considered, it is called substance (jawhar), whether motion actually occurs or not.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage explains two key terms: time (زمان) and duration (دهر) in relation to motion. The original text said something about 'motion occurs in it'. The commentator clarifies: this does not mean the divine essence moves, but rather that the parts of motion have a relation to that substance (the foundation) through its persistence. From this, an imaginary extension (امتداد وهمي) arises, which is called time. If the relation of motion is not considered, the same substance is called dahr (eternity) without temporal extension. Another gloss states that if the occurrence of motion is considered, it is time; if not, it is substance (جوهر). So time is defined as a measure related to motion, while the underlying substance is eternal.

Time (Zaman) and Eternity (Dahr) in Islamic Philosophy
Defines zaman and dahr based on relation to motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between zaman (time) and dahr (eternity) as relational concepts dependent on motion.

Core Idea
Time is an imaginary extension arising from motion; dahr is motionless eternity.

In Islamic philosophy, time is not a self-standing entity but a relation. This passage, from a commentary on al-Mawaqif, explains how the same substrate is called zaman when motion's parts are related to it, and dahr when they are not. It also addresses a key nuance: motion here does not mean literal movement of God, but the continuous relation that generates the 'flowing now'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that the definition hinges on whether motion's parts are related to the substrate.
2
Grasp the core definitions
The passage gives two names for the same substrate: zaman (if motion relation is considered) and dahr (if not).
3
Understand the 'flowing now' analogy
The commentary explains that time emerges from the continuous, unstable 'now' similar to how a flowing stream creates an extension.
This shows time is an imaginary construct, not a real entity.
4
Consider the alternative view
Some say that if motion is observed, it's time; if not, it's substance. This ties time to perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن حصلت الحركة فيه ووجدت لأجزائها نسبة إليه سُمِّي زماناً وإن لم توجد الحركة فيه سُمِّي دهراً. قوله : ( ووجدت إلخ) عطف تفسيري للجملة السابقة يعني : ليس المراد بحصول الحركة فيه بحركة فإنَّ حركة الواجب محال ولذا لم يقل ثم إِنَّ تحرك بل المراد أن يوجد لأجزاء الحركة نسبة إليه باستمراره في جميع الأجزاء حصل منه امتداد وهمي يسمى بالزمان على نحو ما قالوا الآن السيال؛ أنه يفعل باستمراره وعدم استقراره الزمان بمعنى الأمر الممتد. قوله : ( وإن لم توجد إلخ) أي وإن لم تعتبر نسبة الحركة تسمى دهراً. قوله : ( وإن لم توجد الحركة إلخ قيل مرادهم أنه إن لوحظ وقوع الحركة فيه يسمى الزمان، وإن لم يلاحظ يسمى جوهراً سواء وقعت بالفعل أم لا .
Then if motion occurs in it and there is found for its parts a relation to it, it is called time; and if motion is not found in it, it is called dahr. His saying (and found etc.) is an explanatory conjunct to the previous sentence, meaning: the intended meaning of the occurrence of motion in it is not by motion, for motion of the Necessary is impossible, and therefore he did not say 'then it moves'. Rather, the intended meaning is that there exists for the parts of motion a relation to it by its continuity in all parts, from which arises an imaginary extension called time, in the manner of what they said about the flowing Now: that it produces time by its continuity and lack of stability, in the sense of the extended thing. His saying (and if it is not found etc.) means: if the relation of motion is not considered, it is called dahr. His saying (and if motion is not found etc.): it is said their intended meaning is that if the occurrence of motion in it is observed, it is called time; and if it is not observed, it is called substance, whether motion actually occurs or not.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms 'time' (zaman) and 'dahr' (eternity) in the context of the discussion of the substrate (the bearer of motion). It begins by stating that if motion occurs in that substrate and a relation of motion's parts to it is found, then it is called time; if motion is not found, it is called dahr. The commentary explains that 'occurrence of motion' does not mean literal motion, as God is not subject to motion. Rather, it means that the parts of motion are related to the substrate through its continuity, producing an imaginary extension that is called time, analogous to the 'flowing now'. If the relation to motion is not considered, it is called dahr. An alternative view is also mentioned: if motion is observed, it is time; if not, it is a substance (jawhar). This clarifies that time is not an independent entity but a relational concept.

The Nature of Time as Continuous Quantity and Measure of Motion
Al-Jurjani defines time as continuous quantity and proves it is the measure of the fastest circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defines time as a continuous quantity and proves it is the measure of the fastest circular motion.

Core Idea
Time is a continuous measure of non-permanent motion, not a collection of instants.

In this passage, al-Jurjani tackles the reality of time, rejecting a discrete view and establishing it as a continuous quantity. He then proves that time must measure the fastest motion—the daily celestial sphere—because only circular motion avoids the termination inherent in rectilinear motion. The gloss clarifies key points and answers an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation to get a feel for the argument. Then read the literal translation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core definition of time
Locate where al-Jurjani defines time as a continuous quantity (كم متصل) and as a measure (مقدار). Note what he excludes (discrete quantity, permanent things).
3
Follow the proof that time measures circular motion
The argument proceeds: rectilinear motion ceases due to finite dimensions and necessary rest; so time cannot measure it. Therefore time measures circular motion.
Consider why circular motion is perpetual.
4
Understand why the fastest motion is the standard
Time measures all motions, but the fastest motion (daily celestial) has the smallest time, so it serves as the unit. Larger measures are measured by the smaller.
This is like measuring with a standard unit.
5
Review the objection and answer
The gloss answers a potential objection: why must the motion be in place (position) rather than quality? The answer is that qualitative motion also requires rest, so cessation is inevitable.
This strengthens the argument for circular motion.

The Second Observatory - The Eighth Objective: The Reality of Time. So time is not a discrete quantity because that necessitates its composition from successive instants which are the units; rather it is a continuous quantity, so it is a measure, i.e., a continuous magnitude whose parts meet at supposed common boundaries. And it is not a measure of a permanent thing whose parts are gathered; otherwise time would be permanent like it, because the measure of the permanent is necessarily permanent, but time is impossible to be permanent; otherwise successive events would be simultaneous together. So it is a measure of a non-permanent state for the moving body whose existence as moving is inconceivable without time—and that is motion. And its cessation is impossible, i.e., the cessation of the motion of which time is the measure; otherwise time would also cease, necessitating its non-existence after existence, which is impossible by the same proof used to establish the first doctrine. So time is the measure of circular motion, because rectilinear motion necessarily ceases due to the finitude of dimensions. So it is not permissible then for rectilinear motion to go on in a straight line indefinitely, nor is a rest between every two motions differing in direction from a single mover necessary? Wait: the text says 'وجوب سكون' meaning 'and the necessity of rest between every two motions differing in direction emanating from a single mover, so it is not permissible also for the rectilinear motion to continue and endure by the mover turning from its direction to another direction.' And it—i.e., circular motion—is celestial motion. And there is no doubt that all motions differing in speed and slowness are measured by it—i.e., by time; so it is said: this motion, for example, is in an hour, and that in two hours, and so on. Thus time is the measure of the fastest of them, because the fastest of motions has the least measure, i.e., time. His saying: 'for the moving body whose existence etc.' The benefit of this description is to indicate the explanation of the necessity of time for motion so that the necessity of its cessation upon its cessation follows. His saying: 'because rectilinear motion'—he meant it in the conventional sense, which is what moves the body from its place, whether on a straight or curved line. His saying: 'ceases'—and likewise quantitative motion for the mentioned proof, because it is impossible for magnitude to go on indefinitely, and likewise its decrease; only qualitative motion remains, and they did not address the negation of time being its measure because its existence is doubtful due to the lack of proof that transition in it is gradual, as will come, and because the proponents of latency and emergence deny it. His saying: 'from successive instants which are the units'—i.e., on the assumption that time is a discrete quantity. His saying: 'so it is the measure of circular motion'—it is said: from where is it determined that that motion is motion in position? Why may it not be motion in quality? Moreover, the confinement of that non-permanent thing to motion is only known by incomplete induction, so the proof is probable, not demonstrative. The answer to the first is that between every two motions in quality there is also rest, as the author stated in the discussions of place; thus the cessation necessary on the assumption that it is a measure of a motion in place follows. Then the smallness of time necessitates the speed of motion, and then it is possible for all motions to be measured by it because the larger, in terms of measure, is measured by the smaller and not vice versa; it is said: this farsakh is so many ramhs, and this ramh is so many cubits, and this cubit is so many fingers, for the smaller counts the larger due to the larger containing a multiple of the smaller with addition, and the larger does not count the smaller because it is impossible for it to contain a multiple of the larger. And you have known that the fastest of existing motions is the daily motion, which is the motion of the greatest sphere. So time is the measure of the daily motion; thus that motion is measured first and essentially, and the rest of motions secondarily and accidentally. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by defining time as a continuous quantity (كم متصل), not discrete, because discrete quantity would require composition from successive indivisible instants (آنات). The author then specifies that time is a measure (مقدار), i.e., a continuous magnitude whose parts meet at boundaries. It is not a measure of a permanent thing (قار), because that would make time itself permanent, contradicting the nature of time as involving succession. Instead, time is the measure of a non-permanent state (هيئة غير قارة) of a moving body, namely motion. The author argues that the motion of which time is the measure cannot cease, because if the motion ceased, time would cease, leading to timelessness after existence, which is impossible by earlier proof. Therefore, time must be the measure of circular motion (الحركة المستديرة), specifically celestial motion (الحركة الفلكية), because rectilinear motion is finite and necessarily interrupted by rest when direction changes. The passage then explains that all motions are measured by time, and the fastest motion—the daily motion of the celestial sphere—is the standard. Larger measures are measured by smaller, so the fastest motion, having the smallest time, serves as the unit. Hence time is essentially the measure of the daily motion, and other motions are measured secondarily. The author includes a gloss addressing an objection: why must motion be in place (وضع) rather than quality? The answer is that between two qualitative motions there is also rest, so cessation is inevitable. The proof concludes: 'وهو المطلوب' (and that is what was sought).

Key Terms

مقدار
هيئة غير قارة
حركة مستديرة
حركة فلكية
حركة يومية
آنات
قار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is composed of discrete, indivisible instants (like atoms).
Correction: The passage argues that time cannot be a discrete quantity because that would require composition from successive instants, which is problematic. Instead, time is a continuous quantity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that time must be the measure of circular motion rather than rectilinear motion?
A) Because circular motion is faster.
B) Because rectilinear motion is finite and requires rest between changes in direction, leading to cessation.
C) Because circular motion is non-essential.
D) Because time is a discrete quantity.

Key Takeaway
Time is a continuous quantity that measures the fastest circular motion, enabling the measurement of all other motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that time is continuous, not a series of separate instants. In what areas of your life do you treat your days as disconnected 'nows' rather than a flowing whole, and how might that disconnect you from the urgency of your ultimate purpose?
?
If time measures the fastest circular motion, and your life is measured by that same celestial rhythm, how are you spending each rotation—each day—in a way that aligns with the perpetual motion toward your Lord?
?
Time is defined as a measure of motion; what 'motion' are you spending your time on? Are your actions moving you closer to or further from the Gardener of time, and does your daily motion reflect your professed priorities?

Five Objections to Time as Measure of Circular Motion
Al-Jurjani lists five objections to the definition of time as measure of circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five key objections raised against the definition of time as the measure of circular motion, and why each is considered a denial of underlying assumptions.

Core Idea
Time as measure of circular motion rests on five contested assumptions.

Al-Jurjani now presents a series of objections against the earlier definition of time. Each objection targets a fundamental assumption underlying that definition. As you read, pay attention to how each 'denial' (ممنوعة) challenges a different aspect of the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get a sense of the five objections.
Notice that each objection is introduced with ordinal numbering (الأولى, الثاني, etc.)
2
Identify the structure: five numbered objections
List the five objections in your own words. Each one begins with a claim that is 'denied' (ممنوعة).
The objections are not arguments but challenges to assumptions.
3
Analyze each objection's core claim
For each objection, note which assumption about time is being questioned. Why does the opponent think this assumption is unproven or false?
Use the commentary to deepen your understanding.
4
Consider the overall significance
Reflect on how these objections affect the definition of time. Do they completely refute the definition or just show it needs further defense?
This connects to the broader debate about the nature of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه أنه مبني على أمور كلها ممنوعة الأولى كل قابل للتفاوت كم، وإنما يصح أن لو بین أنه قابل للتفاوت لذاته ) ولم يبين ذلك في الزمان ( الثاني امتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ) وإلا لجاز كون الزمان كماً منفصلاً وما استدل به علی امتناع الجزء مردود كما ستعرفه ( الثالث امتناع عدمه ) إذ لو جاز عدم الزمان لجاز أن يكون مقدار الحركة مستقيمة منقطعة ( والدليل ) الذي استدل به على امتناع عدمه قد عرفت ما فيه من الخلل ( الرابع أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً فإنه إذا لم يجب ذلك جاز أن تكون تلك الحركة المستقيمة مستمرة بلا انقطاع على طريقة الرجوع والانعطاف ويكون الزمان مقدارها وما يتمسك به في إثبات السكون بينهما ستقف على فساده ( الخامس أن له ) أي للزمان ( محلاً إما لوجوده أو لعرضيته والأولى أن يترك هذا الترديد ويقال : لوجوده وعرضيته، فإن اقتضاءه محلاً موجوداً يتوقف على وجوده وعرضيته معاً ( ولم يثبتا ) أي لم
And the objection against it is that it is based on matters, all of which are denied. First: everything susceptible to variation is a quantity, but that is only valid if it is established that it is susceptible to variation in itself, and that has not been shown in time. Second: the impossibility of the indivisible part; otherwise it would be permissible for time to be a discrete quantity, and the evidence used for the impossibility of the part is refuted, as you will know. Third: the impossibility of its non-existence; for if non-existence of time were allowed, then it would be permissible for it to be the measure of a rectilinear, interrupted motion, and the proof used for the impossibility of its non-existence—you have known what deficiency it contains. Fourth: that between every two motions there is rest; for if that is not necessary, then it would be permissible for that rectilinear motion to be continuous without interruption by way of return and turning, and time would be its measure, and what is relied upon to prove rest between them—you will see its corruption. Fifth: that it—i.e., time—has a locus, either because of its existence or its accidentality, and it is better to leave this hesitation and say: because of its existence and accidentality together, for its requiring an existing locus depends on both its existence and its accidentality, and neither has been established.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents five objections (اعتراضات) against the previous conclusion that time is the measure of circular motion. The author lists them concisely, indicating each is 'ممنوعة' (denied). First objection: Time is claimed to be a quantity (كم) because it admits variation (تفاوت), but this requires that it is intrinsically variable, which has not been proven. Second objection: The impossibility of an indivisible part (جزء لا يتجزأ) is assumed; if such parts existed, time could be discrete—the alleged proof against indivisible parts is flawed. Third objection: The impossibility of time's non-existence is assumed; if time could cease, it could measure interrupted rectilinear motion. The proof for its necessary existence is weak. Fourth objection: The assertion that there is rest between every two motions is not necessarily true; if not, rectilinear motion could be continuous by turning back, and time could measure that. Fifth objection: Time requires a locus (محل) either due to its existence or its accidentality; the need for a locus depends on both, and neither has been proven. The passage ends abruptly; the commentary likely continues with responses.

امتناع عدم
سكون

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of time as measure of circular motion is obvious and universally accepted.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that this definition relies on several assumptions that have been explicitly contested. The objections demonstrate that the definition is far from self-evident.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objections show that even something as seemingly obvious as time rests on unproven assumptions. What fundamental beliefs about your own life or faith do you hold without ever questioning? Would you be willing to examine one of them today?

If time were not continuous or always present, how would that affect your sense of urgency in turning to Allah? Does your assumption about time's stability make you procrastinate in matters of the afterlife?
?
Each objection denies a matter that was taken for granted. In your spiritual life, is there a 'matter' you deny—like your own helplessness or need for guidance—that if acknowledged would change how you approach your Creator?

The Three Possible Definitions of Place (Makan)
Sharh defines place via three possibilities: surface, imaginary void, or existing incorporeal volume.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible definitions of place (al-makan) and the rational argument behind each.

Core Idea
Place is either a surface, an imaginary void, or an existent volume — only three possibilities.

In this passage, the author examines the concept of place (al-makan) and argues that it must be understood through one of three definitions. He begins with a rational deduction based on the indivisibility of the body that requires place to be either a surface or a volume, then refines that into three concrete possibilities. This foundational discussion clarifies how theologians and philosophers conceive of physical location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, two hypotheses, then three possibilities.
2
Identify the two initial hypotheses
The passage first argues that place must be either a surface (if divided in two directions) or a volume (if divided in all directions). Find these two and note the reasoning.
3
See how volume splits into two
The volume possibility is further divided: either it is an imaginary void (the view of the mutakallimūn) or an existent but non-material volume. Understand why the material volume is rejected.
This gives exactly three possibilities.
4
Understand the commentator's note
Read the commentator's remark about why this clarification was needed. It addresses the concepts of conformity (inbiṭāq) and filling (mal').
5
Summarize the three possibilities
Write down the three definitions: surface, imaginary void, existent incorporeal volume. Explain the reasoning for each in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوضيح ذلك بما لا مزيد عليه أن يقال : لما كان الجسم بكليته في أن يكون المكان أمراً غير منقسم لاستحالة أن يكون المنقسم في جميع جهاته حاصلاً بتمامه لا ينقسم، ولا أن يكون أمراً منقسماً في جهة واحدة فقط. كالخط مثلاً لاستحالة كونه محيطاً بالجسم بكليته، فهو إما منقسم في جهتين أو في الجهات كلها وعلى الأول يكون المكان سطحاً عرضياً لامتناع الجزء وما في حكمه، ولا يجوز أن يكون حالاً في المتمكن لما مر، بل فيما يحويه، ويجب يكون مماساً للسطح الظاهر من المتمكن في جميع جهاته، وإلا لم يكن مالئاً له فهو السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوي، وعلى الثاني يكون المكان بعداً منقسماً في جميع الجهات مساوياً للبعد الذي في الجسم بحيث ينطبق أحدهما على الآخر سارياً فيه بكليته فذلك البعد الذي هو المكان. أما أن يكون أمراً موهوماً يشغله الجسم ويملؤه على سبيل التوهم كما هو مذهب المتكلمين، وإما أن يكون أمراً موجوداً، ولا يجوز أن يكون بعداً مادياً قائماً بالجسم. إذ يلزم من حصول الجسم فيه تداخل الأجسام فهو بعد مجرد فلا مزيد للاحتمالات على الثلاثة هذا ما عليه أهل العلم والتحقيق

قوله : ( وتوضيح إلخ) لما كان في استلزام الانطباق، وكونه مالئاً له لكون الملاقاة بينهما بالتمام فيكون المكان بعداً أو بالمماسة بالأطراف سطحاً خفاء إزالة بالتوضيح المشتمل على الوجه العقلي بحيث لم يبق فيه اشتباه .
And the clarification of that, beyond which there is no more to be added, is to say: Since the body in its entirety [requires] that place be indivisible, because it is impossible for what is divided in all its directions to exist completely without division, nor can it be something divided in only one direction, like a line for example, because it is impossible for it to encompass the body in its entirety. Therefore it is either divided in two directions, or in all directions. On the first [hypothesis], place would be a surface, because a part and what is like it are impossible, and it cannot inhere in the located object, as has passed, but rather in what contains it, and it must be tangent to the apparent surface of the located object in all its directions, otherwise it would not fill it. Thus it is the inner surface of the containing body that is tangent to the apparent surface of the contained. On the second [hypothesis], place would be a volume divided in all directions, equal to the volume that is in the body, such that one conforms to the other, permeating it entirely. Thus that volume is place. It is either something imaginary that the body occupies and fills by way of imagination, as is the doctrine of the theologians, or it is something existent, and it cannot be a material volume subsisting in a body, because that would entail the interpenetration of bodies, so it is an incorporeal volume. Thus the possibilities are no more than three. This is what the people of knowledge and verification hold. Then the commentator said: 'His saying: (And the clarification etc.)' — Since there was in the implication of conformity and its being a filler for it due to the contact between them being complete, making place a volume, or by tangency at the edges making it a surface, an obscurity, [the author] removed it by the clarification containing the rational argument such that no confusion remains in it.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines place (al-makan) by enumerating its three possible definitions. The argument begins by noting that the body in its entirety requires place to be indivisible; it cannot be divisible in all directions because then it would not be entirely contained, nor can it be divisible in only one direction like a line, because a line cannot surround the whole body. Hence place must be divisible in either two directions (a surface) or all directions (a volume). On the first hypothesis, place is a surface: the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body, filling it completely. On the second hypothesis, place is a volume equal to and conforming to the body's volume. Then the text presents the three possibilities: (1) place is a surface, (2) it is an imaginary void (as held by the mutakallimūn), or (3) it is an existent incorporeal volume (not a material volume, which would cause interpenetration). The commentator then explains the need for this clarification: the concepts of conformity (inbiṭāq) and filling (mal') could be obscure, so the author provided a rational argument to remove confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If place is either a surface, an imaginary void, or an incorporeal volume, then where do you truly dwell? Not your physical address, but the place of your soul: is it contained by worldly surfaces, trapped in an imaginary void of illusions, or anchored in an incorporeal reality? Which one does your daily pursuit reveal?
?
The mutakallimun held that place is imaginary yet real enough for bodies to occupy. Your own body occupies space every moment. What would it cost you to shift your sense of 'place' from the imaginary to the real?
?

If place is a surface, you are merely touching the outer edges of faith. If a void, you are filled with distractions. If an incorporeal volume, you are permeated by divine light. Which one are you, and what step will you take right now to become the latter?

The Common Definition of Place: Criticism and Refinement
Text criticizes popular definition, proposes refinement, and labels it a vulgar error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the common definition of place as 'that which prevents descent' is considered a vulgar error in Islamic theology, and how the commentator proposes to refine it.

Core Idea
A place must both support and prevent descent, otherwise moving objects would have no place.

The commentator examines the everyday usage of the word 'place' (makān) among the public. While initially offering a defense based on common parlance, he ultimately shows that the definition is flawed and leads to absurd consequences. This lesson unpacks his critique and the refined understanding required for theological precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the progression from stating the definition to critiquing it.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'descending' (nuzūl) and 'falling' (suqūṭ).
2
Identify the two responses
The commentator first defends the definition by noting it indicates common usage, not a strict definition. Then he proposes a refinement to avoid implications like a rope being a place.
The refinement adds the concept of 'reliance' (iʿtimād).
3
Understand the vulgar error
The commentator argues that the original definition leads to the absurdity that moving objects (like an arrow or bird) have no place. Then he refutes this by noting that motion requires a starting and ending point, which are places.
This shows why the refined definition is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العامة فإنهم يطلقون لفظ المكان على ما يمنع الشيء من النزول

قوله : ( فإنهم يطلقون قد نقلنا فيما سبق من الشفاء أنَّ الأغلب عندهم إطلاق المكان على ما يكون فيه الشيء وإن لم يستقر فيه قوله : ( على ما يمنع الشيء من النزول أي ما يستقر عليه الشيء، ويقع عليه اعتماده والنزول غير السقوط فلا يرد أنه يلزم أن يكون الحبل الذي علق به الحجر من رأسه مكاناً له . وكذا القوة القسرية المصعدة للحجر وليس كذلك على أن المقصود بيان الإطلاق لا التعريف الجامع المانع والدرقة محركة ترس من الجلد ليس فيه خشب ولا عصب

قوله : على ما يمنع الشيء من النزول الأظهر أن يقول ما يعتمد عليه الشيء ويمنعه من النزول إذ الاقتصار على الثاني يوهم أن يكون الحبل الذي علق به الحجر من رأسه مكاناً له عند العامة وكذا القوة القسرية المصعدة للحجر، وليس كذلك واعلم أن جعل المكان عبارة عما ذكر خطأ عامي، لأنه يوجب أن لا يكون السهم النافذ في الهواء والطائر فيما بين السماء والأرض، وكذا الحجر المتحرك بالقسر إلى جهة فوق في مكان إذ ليس لها في تلك الحالة موضع يمنعها من النزول

وهو ممتنع فإنا نشاهد كلاً منها متحركاً والحركة، لا بد أن يكون عن شيء إلى شيء وما منه الانتقال وإليه، هو المكان كذا في الأبكار.

قوله : ( حتى لو وضعت الدرقة الدرقة ترس من الجلد ليس فيه خشب ولا عصب .
As for the common people, they apply the word 'place' to whatever prevents a thing from descending.

His saying: (for they apply — we have previously transmitted from al-Shifa' that the prevalent usage among them is to apply 'place' to whatever a thing is in, even if it does not settle in it). His saying: (on what prevents the thing from descending) i.e., what the thing rests upon and receives its reliance. And 'descending' is not the same as 'falling', so it is not objected that this would entail that the rope from which a stone is hung is its place. Similarly for the coercive force that lifts the stone upward, it is not so, since the purpose is to indicate the usage, not a comprehensive and restrictive definition. And 'al-daraqah' (with vowel marks) is a shield made of leather, with no wood or sinew.

His saying: (on what prevents the thing from descending — the more apparent is that he say: what the thing relies upon and prevents it from descending, because confining it to the second [part] gives the impression that the rope from which a stone is hung is its place according to the common people, and similarly the coercive force that lifts the stone upward, and it is not so. And know that making 'place' an expression for what was mentioned is a vulgar error, because it entails that an arrow piercing the air, a bird flying between heaven and earth, and likewise a stone moved coercively upward, have no place, since in that state they have no position that prevents them from descending — which is impossible, for we observe each of them moving, and movement must be from something to something, and that from which and to which the transition occurs is place. Thus it is [stated] in al-Abkar.

His saying: (even if the daraqah were placed) — al-daraqah is a shield of leather with no wood or sinew.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage critically examines the common people's definition of place as 'that which prevents a thing from descending'. The commentator begins by noting that in al-Shifā', the prevalent usage among common people is actually broader: they apply 'place' to whatever contains a thing, even if the thing does not settle in it. The author then refines the definition: 'what prevents descent' means what the thing rests upon and relies on. 'Descent' (nuzūl) is distinguished from 'falling' (suqūṭ). Using this definition does not lead to absurdities like making a hanging rope the place of the stone. The commentator then suggests a better phrasing: 'what the thing relies upon and prevents it from descending', because the shorter phrase might wrongly imply that a rope or a coercive upward force is a place. He then declares the common definition a vulgar error (khaṭa' 'āmmī) because it would deny place to moving objects like arrows, birds, or stones thrown upward, since nothing prevents them from descending in that state. This is impossible because we observe them moving, and all motion requires a starting point and an endpoint, both of which are places. The passage ends with a note on the word 'daraqah' (a leather shield).

Correction: The passage shows this definition is a vulgar error because it would deny place to moving objects, which is impossible.

D) The common people do not use it

Key Takeaway
The common definition of place is insufficient; a correct definition must account for both prevention of descent and support, otherwise moving objects would be placeless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen how a common definition, taken for granted, can lead to contradiction. Is there any belief or practice in your own understanding of Islam that you hold simply because 'that's how it's commonly understood,' without checking whether it really coheres with the deeper principles? Will you examine it today?
?
The author called the common definition a 'vulgar error'—not just a mistaken but a coarse, unrefined error. Consider your own intellectual habits: Where do you settle for vulgar understandings of complex truths? Are you willing to endure the discomfort of refining your definitions, even when they challenge what everyone says?
?
The passage shows that even good intentions (preventing descent) can lead to a defective definition if they overlook motion and reliance. In your life, have you ever failed to see that your own good intentions were incomplete because you ignored the dynamic reality around you? What step will you take this week to correct that oversight?

Place as Self-Subsistent Dimension Necessitating Location
Sanusi defines place and argues only dimensions necessitate location and prevent interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in classical kalām, place is defined as a self-subsistent dimension that alone necessitates location and prevents interpenetration, while matter and corporeal form do not.

Core Idea
Only dimensions, not matter or form, necessitate location and prevent interpenetration.

Sanusi begins the ninth topic by defining place (al-makan) as a type of continuous quantity. He then identifies what it is that truly necessitates location (al-hayyiz) and prevents interpenetration. By examining why matter and corporeal form cannot be the cause, he isolates the dimension (al-buʿd) as the sole necessitator. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding physical extension in kalām cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: definition → exclusion of matter → exclusion of corporeal form → conclusion about dimensions.
2
Identify the three candidates for location
The passage considers matter, corporeal form, and dimension as candidates for 'what is essentially located'. For each, note whether the author affirms or denies it and the reason given.
Matter has no magnitude; corporeal form can persist through volume changes (rarefaction/condensation).
3
Understand the argument for dimensions
Why do dimensions necessitate location and prevent interpenetration? The passage simply states it: only dimensions do so. Reflect on why this is foundational for continued discussion of place.
This connects to the logical conclusion: if dimensions could interpenetrate, bodies could too.
4
Test with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you have grasped the main point. The question asks what necessitates location according to this passage.
Only dimensions (C) is correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل. والمتحيز بالذات هو البعد دون المادة إذ لا مقدار لها في ذاتها فلا تكون مقتضية للحيز، ودون الصورة الجسمية لأنَّ الجسم الواحد قد يتخلخل فيشغل مكاناً كبيراً ثم يتكاثف فيشغل مكاناً صغيراً مع بقاء صورته الجسمية في الحالين. فليست الصورة الجسمية في ذاتها مقتضية للحيز وعدم اقتضاء سائر الصور والأعراض سوى الأبعاد للحيز ظاهر فليس المقتضى للحيز وامتناع التداخل في الأجسام المشاهدة إلا الأبعاد فإذا لم يمتنع تداخلها لم يمتنع تداخل الأجسام أيضاً.
The second observation - the ninth topic: Place, which is of continuous quantity. That which is essentially located (al-mutaḥayyiz bi-al-dhāt) is the dimension (al-buʿd), not matter, for matter has no magnitude in itself and thus is not a necessitator of location; nor the corporeal form, because one body may rarefy and occupy a large place then condense and occupy a small place while its corporeal form remains in both states. Thus the corporeal form in itself is not a necessitator of location, and the non-necessitation of location by the other forms and accidents besides dimensions is obvious. So the necessitator of location and the impossibility of interpenetration in observed bodies is only dimensions; if their interpenetration is not impossible, then interpenetration of bodies also is not impossible.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of 'place' (al-makan) and argues that the only thing that necessitates location (al-hayyiz) and prevents interpenetration of bodies is the dimension (al-buʿd) that subsists by itself. The author first identifies place as a type of continuous quantity. He then states that the essentially located thing (al-mutaḥayyiz bi-al-dhāt) is the dimension itself, not matter (since matter has no magnitude in itself) and not the corporeal form (since one body can occupy different volumes while retaining the same form, as in rarefaction and condensation). Therefore, the cause of location and the impossibility of interpenetration among observed bodies is only dimensions. The conclusion follows: if interpenetration of dimensions were possible, then interpenetration of bodies would also be possible.

Al-Jurjani defines the second theory of place.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Place is not just a surface; it is an innate dimension that intuition recognizes naturally.

In the debate about the nature of place, al-Jurjani presents a second possibility: place is an existing dimension that the body enters, not just the surface of another body. This dimension is called 'innate' because human innate intuition was created upon it, meaning it is a basic, natural concept. The passage defines this view succinctly, though it is cut off.

Definition of al-Hayz (Position) and its Relation to Place
Defines al-hayz, explains its generality, and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-hayz (position) as that by which bodies are distinguished in sensory indication, and how it is more general than place.

Core Idea
Al-hayz is broader than physical location—it includes any sensory distinguishability.

In this passage, the author defines al-hayz, a foundational concept for discussing bodies, space, and motion. You'll see how it differs from ordinary 'place' and why the outermost sphere, with no place, still has al-hayz. The commentary then clarifies the two meanings of al-wad' (position) and resolves an objection about rotating bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the first sentence where al-hayz is defined as 'that by which bodies are distinguished in sensory indication.' Note that this definition emphasizes distinguishability, not physical containment.
The term 'sensory indication' (al-ishara al-hissiyya) means pointing or referring to something with the senses.
2
Understand why al-hayz is more general than place
The passage explains that al-hayz includes the outermost sphere (al-muhaddad), which has no place but is still distinguishable. Ask yourself: Why can something have al-hayz without having a place?
Place (al-makan) typically implies containment by another body, whereas al-hayz only requires being distinguishable in spatial reference.
3
Examine the two meanings of al-wad'
The passage quotes al-Tusi to clarify that 'wad'' here refers to the internal configuration of a body, not the categorical ‘position’ in Aristotle's list. It also mentions two meanings: the distinguishing state and the category. Compare them.
4
Follow the objection and response
The objection suggests that if al-hayz is the distinguishing state, it might be considered natural. The response specifies that the claim 'every body has al-hayz' applies only to bodies with a place, excluding the outermost sphere. Then it addresses rotating parts: hypothetical parts have no motion; actual parts change position relative to fixed points.
This shows the careful logic used to avoid contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل: إنَّ الحيز عندهم ما به تتمايز الأجسام في الإشارة الحسية وهو أعم من المكان لتناوله الوضع الذي يمتاز به المحدد عن غيره في الإشارة فهو متحيز وليس في مكان ولا بعد في أن تكون الحالة التي تميزه في الإشارة الحسية عن غيره طبيعية له وإن لم يكن شيء من أوضاعه هي مکان باعتبار تفاوت الأجسام التي يتجدد تباعدها حتى لو فرض عدم تلك الأجسام انتفى التفاوت والتقدير. قوله : ( ما به تتمايز (إلخ أي تكون الإشارة الحسية إلى أحدها غير الإشارة إلى الآخر. قوله : : ( وهو أعم من (المكان قال المحقق الطوسي في شرح الإشارات: إن الوضع ها هنا هو الهيئة العارضة للجسم بسبب نسبة بعض أجزائه إلى بعض لا الذي هو المقولة. أعني ما الزمان منع كون قبول الزيادة والنقصان من عوارض الموجود، ألا يرى أن ما بين الطوفان وبين سيدنا محمد عليه السلام أقل مما بين يومنا ويوم الطوفان . قوله : ( لتناوله الوضع إلخ) في سياق كلامه إشارة إلى أن للوضع معنيين . الحالة التي يتميز بها الجسم في الإشارة الحسية والمقولة التي هي إحدى الأجناس العالية كما مر فإن قلت : إذا فهو ونسبته بالقياس إلى ما تحته أمراً طبيعياً وأيضاً لهم أن يخصوا قولهم : كل جسم متحيز بالأجسام التي لها مكان فيخرج عنه ما لا مكان له وأن يقولوا : إِنَّ المشار إليه بهنا وهناك قد يكون الحالة المميزة في الإشارة الحسية، وحينئذ تندفع المناقضة أيضاً، وأما حديث أجزاء المتحرك بالاستدارة فنقول : إن كانت تلك الأجزاء مفروضة، فلا يعرض لها حركة خارجية قطعاً . وإن كانت موجودة بالفعل كالكواكب المنفصلة عن أجرام الأفلاك المركوزة هي فيها فالمعلوم من حالها بالضرورة تبدل أوضاعها بالقياس إلى الأمور الثابتة تبعاً للحركة الوضعية الحاصلة للفلك. وأما انتقالها من مكان إلى المكان فليس مما علم بالضرورة
And it has been said: Indeed, al-hayz (position) according to them is that by which bodies are distinguished in sensory indication (al-ishara al-hissiyya). It is more general than place (al-makan) because it includes the position (al-wad') by which the outermost sphere is distinguished from other things in indication, so it has al-hayz but is not in a place. And there is no difficulty that the state that distinguishes it in sensory indication from others is natural to it, even if none of its positions are a place, considering the variation of bodies whose distance changes, such that if those bodies were assumed non-existent, the variation and estimation would cease. His statement: 'By which they are distinguished (etc.)' means that the sensory indication to one of them is different from the indication to the other. His statement: 'And it is more general than place' – said al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi in Sharh al-Isharat: Indeed, al-wad' here is the configuration (al-hay'a) that accrues to the body due to the relation of some of its parts to others, not the category (al-maqula). I mean that time prevents the acceptance of increase and decrease from being accidents of the existent. Do you not see that what is between the flood and our master Muhammad, peace be upon him, is less than what is between our day and the day of the flood? His statement: 'Because it includes al-wad' (etc.)' in the context of his speech indicates that al-wad' has two meanings: the state by which the body is distinguished in sensory indication, and the category which is one of the highest genera as mentioned. If you say: then it and its relation to what is below it is a natural matter. Also, they may specify their statement: 'Every body has a position (mutahayyiz)' to bodies that have a place, thus excluding what has no place. And they may say: 'Indeed, what is pointed to by here and there may be the distinguishing state in sensory indication.' Then the contradiction is also repelled. As for the discussion of the parts of the body moving with rotation, we say: if those parts are hypothetical, then they certainly do not undergo external motion. And if they actually exist, like the planets separate from the celestial bodies in which they are fixed, then what is known of their state necessarily is that their positions change relative to fixed things following the positional motion that occurs to the sphere. But their transfer from place to place is not known necessarily.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-hayz' (position) as that by which bodies are distinguished in sensory indication (al-ishara al-hissiyya). It is more general than 'place' (al-makan) because it includes the 'wad'' (position) of the outermost sphere (al-muhaddad), which has no place but still has al-hayz. The commentator then explains the definition: sensory indication to one body is different from indication to another. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi clarifies that 'wad'' here refers to the configuration arising from the relation of parts within the body, not the Aristotelian category. He gives an example about time to illustrate that increase and decrease are not accidental. Another commentator notes that 'wad'' has two meanings: the distinguishing state and the category. An objection is raised: if al-hayz is the distinguishing state, why isn't that sufficient for all bodies? The answer is that the relation of the body to what is beneath it is natural, and proponents can restrict their claim to bodies that have a place, excluding the outermost sphere. They can also say that the pointing 'here and there' refers to the distinguishing state, thus resolving the contradiction. Further, regarding the parts of a rotating body: if the parts are hypothetical, they undergo no external motion; if actual (like planets in spheres), they change positions relative to fixed points due to the sphere's rotational motion, but their transfer from place to place is not a matter of necessary knowledge.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that al-hayz is more general than place; the outermost sphere has al-hayz but no place. Al-hayz only requires that a body be distinguishable in sensory indication, not that it be contained.

Al-Razi's Proof for Planar Sub-Surfaces
Al-Razi argues for the necessity of planar sub-surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that any smooth surface must ultimately consist of planar sub-surfaces to avoid an infinite regress of angles.

Core Idea
Smooth surfaces avoid infinite angles by ending in planar sub-surfaces.

Al-Razi tackles a puzzle about the nature of surfaces: if a surface is smooth (level), its parts cannot differ in height or have pores. But if they differ in height, that unevenness must be composed of small surfaces meeting at angles. To avoid an infinity of angles, those small surfaces must eventually be planar. This lesson walks through that reasoning and the accompanying commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the interplay between Al-Razi's original text and the commentator's glosses.
2
Identify the two causes of unevenness
Al-Razi divides lack of evenness into two possible causes: differences in height and pores. Understand each.
3
Trace the argument for the first cause
Follow the logic: differences in height imply angular connections, which must terminate in planar surfaces to avoid infinite angles.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Understand the second cause and its refutation
See why the pore explanation is rejected: it would require an absurd composition of scattered points.
5
Reflect on the commentary
The commentator clarifies key terms and explains the two interpretations of 'going to infinity.'
This helps resolve potential ambiguities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل. الصفحة ملساء. قال الإمام الرازي في الأربعين عدم الاستواء في السطح إما بسبب اختلاف أجزائه في الارتفاع والانخفاض أو بسبب حصول المسام فيه، أما الأول فلا بد أن يكون بسبب سطوح صغار يتصل بعضها ببعض لا على الاستقامة، بل على الزاوية ولا بد من الانتهاء إلى سطوح صغار مستوية وإلا لذهبت الزاوية إلى غير النهاية وهو محال . وأما حصول المسام في أجزاء السطح فإنه وإن جاز إلا أنه لا بد أن يحصل بين كل منفذين سطح متصل، وإلا لزم كون السطح مركباً من نقط متفرقة، وذلك محال فوجب القول بسطوح مستوية ) ولا يمتنع مماستها ، لمثلها وإلا لم يكن التماس. قوله : ( والثاني (باطل لأنه يستلزم في الجسم اشتمال المتناهي أعني الزاوية على أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل متميزة بعضها عن بعض في الخارج وإن لم تكن منفكة . قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي الفرق بين التوجيهين أن مبنى التوجيه الأول أن المراد بذهاب الزوايا إلى غير النهاية ذهاب كل واحدة منها في الانقسام إلى غير النهاية . ومبنى هذا التوجيه أنَّ المراد بذهاب جميع الزوايا في العدد إلى غير النهاية مع تحققها في الصفحة بالفعل. قوله : ( لا على الاستقامة) أي على وضع واحد سواء كانت مستقيمة أو مستديرة كما يدل عليه الإضراب . قوله : ( وهو محال ) إذ وجود الزوايا الغير المتناهية في السطح المتناهي محال بالضرورة . قوله: (مستوية) أي متصلة لا انخفاض ولا ارتفاع فيها .
The second observation - the ninth intention: Place, which is of continuous quantity. The surface is smooth. Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba'in: The lack of evenness in a surface is either due to differences of its parts in height and depth or due to the occurrence of pores in it. As for the first, it must be due to small surfaces connected to each other not in a straight line but at an angle, and it is necessary to end at small planar surfaces, otherwise the angle would go to infinity, which is impossible. As for the occurrence of pores in the parts of the surface, although it is possible, yet there must occur between every two openings a continuous surface, otherwise the surface would be composed of scattered points, which is impossible. So it is necessary to say there are planar surfaces, and it is not impossible for them to be in contact with each other, otherwise there would be no contact. His saying: (And the second) is invalid because it entails in the body the finite containing, meaning the angle, parts actually infinite distinct from each other in external existence even if not separated. His saying: (Imam al-Razi said: the difference between the two interpretations is that the basis of the first interpretation is that what is meant by the angles going to infinity is each one of them going to infinity in division. And the basis of this interpretation is that what is meant by all the angles going to infinity in number while they actually exist in the surface. His saying: (not in a straight line) meaning in one position, whether straight or curved, as the disjunction indicates. His saying: (and it is impossible) because the existence of infinite angles in a finite surface is necessarily impossible. His saying: (planar) meaning continuous with no depth or height in it.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a proof and its commentary. It begins by stating the topic: 'Place' as a continuous quantity. The smooth surface is introduced. Al-Razi argues that lack of evenness in a surface arises from two causes: (1) differences in elevation of its parts, (2) pores. For the first cause, he argues that it must be due to small surfaces meeting at angles rather than straight lines; otherwise, the angle would recede indefinitely, which is absurd. Hence, after a finite number of such angular connections, we must arrive at small planar surfaces that are not further angled. The commentator then glosses various parts: the second cause is invalid because it would entail an actual infinity of parts within a finite angle; there are two interpretations of what 'going to infinity' means—either each angle divides infinitely, or the number of angles is infinite; 'not in a straight line' means any single orientation, straight or curved; the impossibility is self-evident; 'planar' means continuous without concavity or convexity.

Key Terms
صفحة ملساء
زاوية
سطوح مستوية
غير متناهية
تماس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that all surfaces are planar.
Correction: Al-Razi only argues that smooth surfaces must be composed of planar sub-surfaces to avoid logical absurdity; the overall surface may appear curved or irregular.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi argue that a smooth surface must be composed of planar sub-surfaces?
A) Because pores force it
B) To avoid an infinite regress of angles
C) Because all surfaces are naturally planar
D) Because the philosophers claimed it

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument establishes that smooth surfaces, despite appearances, must be made up of tiny planar segments to avoid a logical absurdity of infinite angles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may present a smooth exterior to others, but inside there are angles and pores. Al-Razi argues that even the smoothest surface must be composed of planar sub-surfaces to avoid absurdity. What 'small planar surfaces' of honesty and sincerity do you need to lay down in your life to avoid the infinite regression of hypocrisy?
?
Al-Razi's proof shows that without a foundation of planar surfaces, the surface would be impossible. In your faith, have you built on flimsy angles—excuses, compromises—that if traced back would lead to an infinite regress? Where do you need to stop and establish a foundational planar truth that cannot be further divided?
?
The impossibility of actual infinites in a finite surface forces a return to basic units. In your spiritual life, have you let small sins accumulate, thinking they are just 'potential'? What actual sin are you tolerating that, if allowed to multiply, would make your faith surface unsteady?

Contact of Smooth Surfaces and Infinite Angles
The commentator discusses the possibility of contact between smooth surfaces and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator reinterprets al-Razi's term 'planar surfaces' to mean 'surfaces without angles,' resolving the problem of infinite angles.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's 'planar' means 'without angle,' not geometrically flat.

This passage tackles a subtle problem in kalam physics: how two smooth surfaces can contact without generating an infinite number of angles. The commentator, following al-Razi, faces an objection about the necessity of ending at planar surfaces. He offers a crucial reinterpretation that saves the argument by redefining 'planar' in terms of angle absence rather than geometric flatness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Note the two main sections: the first on the possibility of contact, the second on the infinite angles problem.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail initially.
2
Identify the core problem
The commentator highlights a problem: if contact requires planar surfaces, and angles are infinite, the argument might fail. Understand why infinite angles are problematic.
Infinite angles violate the impossibility of actual infinity in kalam.
3
Study the reinterpretation
The commentator suggests that by 'planar' al-Razi meant 'without angle,' not 'straight.' This allows curved surfaces (like nested bowls) to work.
This reinterpretation avoids the need for geometrically flat surfaces.
4
Examine the bowl example
The example of two nested bowls demonstrates that lifting the top one creates a void, proving the possibility of contact without requiring strictly planar surfaces.
This example shows the argument works even with curved surfaces.

His saying: (Otherwise there would be no contact, etc.) It is not hidden that the possibility of contact between two surfaces is intuitive, and what he mentioned in its explanation is problematic, because if what is meant by it is contact between them by indivisible parts such that there is no between them... this? His saying: (He said, the Imam, etc.) It is apparent that the problematic aspect in what the Imam mentioned is the non-finiteness of angles in number, and in what the commentator mentioned is the non-division of a single angle actually to infinity. But in his statement: 'It is necessary to end at planar surfaces' there is investigation: why is it not allowed to end at small curved surfaces without ending at planar surfaces, and the angles would not go to infinity? It was said that the commentator only changed the method due to this disruption. And it is possible to direct the Imam's words after conceding that the curved surface has no angle, that by 'planar' he meant what has no angle, by contextual evidence, not what is opposite to curvature; in this sense the desired is obtained, because if we assume two bowls, one placed inside the other, and then the upper one is lifted suddenly, a void occurs. If you say: If the desired is obtained by this, the demonstration of the possibility of the smooth surface becomes superfluous. I say: The Imam did not mention in al-Mulakhkhas the smooth surface in the sense mentioned here, but rather said: 'A surface when it meets another surface and then is raised from it suddenly, etc.' So if he had mentioned it in al-Arba'in as a heading of investigation, it would be permissible to say: Another meaning of his words is that if evenness is not realized in the surface due to connection of its surfaces at an angle, then it is necessary to end at small surfaces with no angle. And by this the desired is established even if it is not a smooth surface, and this is a correct meaning. So contemplate.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two 'قوله' sections. The first comments on the phrase 'otherwise there would be no contact,' arguing that the possibility of contact between two smooth surfaces is self-evident, but what al-Razi said in explanation is problematic. The second section comments on 'Imam al-Razi said' and discusses the difference between two interpretations of the infinite angles: either each angle is infinitely divisible, or the number of angles is infinite. The commentator then raises a doubt: why must the chain end at planar surfaces rather than curved surfaces without angles? He suggests that the commentator (perhaps al-Jurjani?) changed the method to avoid this problem. He then reinterprets al-Razi's 'planar' as meaning 'without angle' (not necessarily straight), which allows the argument to work even with curved surfaces. He gives the example of two nested bowls: lifting the top one suddenly creates a void, proving the point without needing a perfectly smooth planar surface. The objection that this makes the demonstration of the smooth surface superfluous is answered by noting that al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas did not define the smooth surface as was done here.

Definition of Place and Motion's Non-specific Time
Sanusi defines place and refutes a potential objection about motion's necessary time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of place as a continuous quantity and why motion, in its essence, does not necessitate a specific duration or distance.

Core Idea
Motion does not demand a fixed time; it only requires time in general.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a fundamental issue in natural philosophy: what is place and what does motion entail? He defines place as a type of continuous quantity and argues that motion, by its intrinsic nature, does not require a specific amount of time or distance. Instead, it only requires absolute time and distance that exist in every part. This counters a potential objection about the fastest motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the logical flow from definition to argument.
2
Identify the definition of place
Find how Sanusi defines place in the opening sentence. What type of quantity does he say it belongs to?
The definition is: 'Place is of continuous quantity.'
3
Understand the argument about motion and time
Notice the claim that motion does not entail a specific time or distance by its essence. Why does the author reject the necessity of a fastest motion?
The author is avoiding a problematic implication that would require the fastest motion.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل تقع في أي جزء كان من الأجزاء المفروضة للزمان والمسافة فلا تقتضي الحركة لذاتها قدرا معينا من الزمان ولا من المسافة بل تقتضي مطلق الزمان والمسافة الموجودة في كل جزء من أجزائها فلا حاجة بنا إلى دعوى أنَّ اقتضاء الحركة لذاتها زماناً يستلزم أسرع الحركات حتى نحتاج في إبطال اللازم إلى بيان وقوع الحركة في نصف زمان الأسرع مع اتحاد المسافة. وإن لم نسلم نفي الجزء كان هو الجواب في الحقيقة ( وأيضاً فإِنَّ (الكلام من المعترض إنما هو ( في تلك الحركة المخصوصة لا في مطلق الحركة ) أي ليس اعتراضه بأن ماهية الحركة من حيث هي تقتضي زماناً . حتى يدفع بأنه باطل إما لاستلزامه وجود أسرع الحركات أو لأن ماهية الحركة موجودة في ضمن أي جزء من الحركة يوجد في أي جزء كان من أجزاء الزمان على ما قررناه . بل بأن الحركة المخصوصة التي توجد في مسافة مخصوصة تقتضي ذلك، إذ هي باعتبار القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك والمسافة المعينة تقتضي قدراً من الزمان. فإن بديهة العقل تحكم بذلك مع قطع النظر عن معاوقة المخروط، ثم إنَّ الزمان يزداد بسبب قوله: (ونحن نقول الخ) إثبات لعدم اقتضاء ماهية الحركة قدراً من الزمان بحيث لا يرد بحث المصنف . ( قوله : ( كان هو الجواب في (الحقيقة لأن الحركة الخلائية والملائية حينئذ كلتاهما واقعتان في الآن والتفاوت بينهما بقلة السكنات المتخللة وكثرتها فإن الحركة عند أصحاب الجزء هو الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني والأجزاء والآنات والأكوان عندهم متتالية . قوله : ( بأن الحركة المخصوصة (الخ يعني فمعنى قوله لذاتها مع قطع النظر مع المعاوق لا لماهيتها . قوله : ( باعتبار القوة المحركة) بحسب اشتدادها وضعفها والجسم المتحرك باعتبار عظم مقداره وصغره وباختلاف شكله فإنَّ المربع إذا تحرك سطحه كان أبطأ من المخروط إذا تحرك المخروط . قوله : (ثم إنَّ الزمان يزداد الخ أقول : كما أنه يزداد الزمان بازدياد المعاوقة ينقص بانتقاصها ففي مراتب انتقاص المعاوقة، إما أن يمكن معاوقة يكون زمان حركتها مساوياً لحركة قوله : ( وإن لم نسلم نفي الجزء كان هو الجواب لأن مبنى الاستدلال على أنَّ الحركة لا ) تقع إلا على مسافة منقسمة ليثبت كونها في زمان البتة. وقد أشرنا فيما سبق إلى أن القائلين بالجزء لا يشترطون المسافة في الحركة بل إذا انتقل جزء من مكانه إلى جزء آخر يليه تتحقق الحركة. ولذا قالوا الخروج من الحيز السابق عين الدخول في اللاحق كما سيحققه الشارح في مباحث الأكوان، وبالجملة على تقدير ثبوت الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لا يقوم دليل على امتناع خلاء يوازيه مع أن المدعى هو السلب الكلي. أعني امتناع جميع أفراد الخلاء إلا أن يثبت أن إمكان الفرد من الخلاء يستلزم إمكان جميع أفراده.
The second observatory - the ninth topic: Place, which is of continuous quantity. It occurs in any part of the assumed parts of time and distance, so motion does not entail by its essence a specific amount of time nor distance, but rather entails absolute time and distance existing in every part of its parts. Thus we have no need to claim that motion's entailing time by its essence necessitates the fastest of motions, such that we need in refuting the consequent to demonstrate the occurrence of motion in half the time of the fastest with the unity of distance. And if we do not accept the negation of the [indivisible] part, then that is the answer in reality. (And also, the objector's speech is only about that specific motion, not about absolute motion.) That is, his objection is not that the essence of motion as such entails time, so that it would be repelled as false either because it necessitates the existence of the fastest motion or because the essence of motion exists within any part of motion that occurs in any part of time as we have established. Rather, that the specific motion which occurs in a specific distance entails that, for it, by virtue of the motive force, the moving body, and the specific distance, entails an amount of time. For the primary intellect judges that, disregarding the resistance of the cone. Then time increases due to... His statement: 'And we say, etc.' is a proof of the non-entailment of a specific amount of time by the essence of motion such that the author's investigation would not be refuted. His statement: 'It was the answer in reality because the motion in void and in plenum then both occur in an instant, and the difference between them is by the fewness and multiplicity of intervening rests. For motion according to the proponents of the part is the second existence in the second place, and parts, instants, and existences according to them are successive.' His statement: 'As for the specific motion, etc.' means that the meaning of his statement 'by its essence' is with disregard of resistance, not for its quiddity. His statement: 'By virtue of the motive force' according to its intensity and weakness, and the moving body according to the greatness of its size and smallness, and by difference of its shape, for if a square moves its surface, it is slower than a moving cone. His statement: 'Then time increases, etc.' I say: Just as time increases with increase of resistance, it decreases with its decrease. So in the degrees of decrease of resistance, either a resistance is possible whose time of motion equals that of motion... His statement: 'And if we do not accept the negation of the part, then that is the answer, because the basis of the inference is that motion does not occur except on a divisible distance to prove that it is in time absolutely. And we have indicated previously that the proponents of the part do not require distance in motion; rather, if a part transfers from its place to the adjacent part, motion is realized. Hence they said: leaving the previous space is identical to entering the next, as the commentator will verify in the discussions of existences. In summary, on the assumption of the existence of the indivisible part, no proof stands for the impossibility of a void equal to it, while the claim is universal negation, i.e., the impossibility of all individuals of the void, unless it is proven that the possibility of one individual of the void necessitates the possibility of all its individuals.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a combination of definition, argument, and response to an objection. It begins by defining place (المكان) as a type of continuous quantity. The author then argues that motion in its essence does not require a specific amount of time or distance; rather, it only requires absolute time and distance, which exist in every part. This is to counter the claim that entailing time would necessitate a fastest motion. The objector is then addressed: the objection is about specific motion, not absolute motion. Specific motion, considering the motive force, moving body, and distance, does necessitate a specific time by intuitive judgment, even without resistance. The commentary then explains that time may increase due to resistance. The author further argues that if one does not accept the negation of the indivisible part (جزء لا يتجزأ), then the answer is that according to proponents of the part, motion in void and plenum occur in an instant, differing only by the number of intervening rests. Finally, he summarizes that on the assumption of indivisible parts, no proof can show the impossibility of a void equal to it, unless one proves that the possibility of one void individual implies all.

الحركة

Correction: Motion by its essence does not entail a specific amount of time or distance; it only requires absolute time and distance. Specific time arises from external factors like motive force, body, and distance.
