Definition of al-Kayf and Its Features
Definition of quality and conditions for definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of quality as an accident that does not entail division or non-division in a primary way, and why it is considered an incomplete description.

Core Idea
Quality is defined by what it does not entail, and cannot be fully defined.

This lesson focuses on the definition of quality (al-kayf) as presented in the third observatory of the text. The author defines it as a specific kind of accident with careful restrictions to distinguish it from other categories. You will also learn why this definition is an incomplete description (rasm naqis) and the conditions for proper definitions of highest genera.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its context
Begin by reading the passage that defines quality as an accident. Notice that the definition includes three key restrictions: does not entail division, does not entail non-division, and this entailment is primary. Understand that these guards exclude other categories like quantity, unity, and point.
2
Understand why it is an incomplete description
The text states that this definition is a rasm naqis (incomplete description) because quality belongs to the highest genera, which cannot be fully defined due to their simplicity. Reflect on why highest genera cannot have proper definitions involving genus and differentia.
This is a key point in kalam logic.
3
Examine the conditions for correct definitions
The passage discusses that definitions must use terms clearer than the definiendum. It gives examples of invalid definitions: substance defined as not accident (equal clarity) and quantity defined by negating all other categories (not clearer). Apply this condition to the definition of quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث في الكيفيات ] قدم مباحث الكيف على سائر المقولات لأنه أصح وجوداً من جميعها إذ منه المحسوسات التي هي أظهر الموجودات إلا أنه قدم الكم عليها لما الماديات والمجردات ) وفيه مقدمة وفصول ) أربعة . [ المقصد الأول في تعريفه وأقسامه ] مر من أنه يعم الأولية ( أما تعريفه فإنه عرض لا يقضي القسمة واللاقسمة اقتضاء أولياً) أي بالذات ومن غير واسطة ) ولا يكون معناه معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير وهذا ( التعريف (رسم ناقص) للكيف ( وهو الغاية في الأجناس العالية ) فإنها لبساطتها على القول بامتناع تركبها من أمور متساوية لا تحد أصلاً ولا ترسم رسماً تاماً . ( ويجوز ) تعريفها الرسمي ( بالأمور الوجودية والعدمية) أيضاً ( بشرط أن تكون تلك الأمور (أجلى) مما يعرف بها من الأجناس العالية ( فلا يصح أن يقال : ( مثلاً ( الجوهر ما ليس بعرض ) فإن الجوهر والعرض يتساويان في المعرفة ،والجهالة فلا يجوز ذكر أحدهما في تعريف الآخر (و) لا أن يقال : (الكم ما ليس بكيف ولا أين إلى آخر المقولات ) . لأنها ليست أجلى من الكم حتى تؤخذ في تعريفه فقولنا: عرض يتناول الأعراض كلها ( واحترزنا بقولنا : لا يقتضي القسمة عن الكم فإنه يقتضي القسمة لذاته ( وبقولنا : ) ولا يقتضي ( اللاقسمة عن الوحدة والنقطة ) المقتضيتين لها (عند من قال : إنهما من الأعراض ) أي على القول بأنهما موجودتان في الخارج وأما على القول الأمور الاعتبارية، فلا حاجة إلى هذا القيد لعدم دخولهما في العرض مرت إليه الإشارة ( و ) بقولنا (اقتضاء أولياً عن خروج العلم بمعلوم واحد ) هو بسيط بأنهما من • قوله : (لا تحد أصلاً لا تاماً ولا ناقصاً لوجوب ذكر الجنس فيها ولا جنس لها . قوله : ( والعدمية) كالتعريف المذكور. كما قوله : (لا يقتضي القسمة أي قبول القسمة الفرضية لأن الكم لا يقتضي نفس القسمة إذ يجوز أن لا يفرضها الفارض. وقد سبق من المصنف أن قبولها لا ينافي فعليتها قوله : ( وبقولنا اقتضاء أولياً الخ) قيل : تبعية الشارح للمصنف في جعل الأولية قيداً لمطلق الاقتضاء من غير تعرض لما عليه يدل على ارتضائه وجوب تعلق القيد المذكور بذلك المطلق
The third observatory on qualities. He presented the discussions of quality before the other categories because it is more sound in existence than all of them, since from it come the sensible things which are the most apparent of existents. However, he presented quantity before it because of material and abstract things. And it contains an introduction and four chapters. The first section on its definition and divisions. It was mentioned that it includes the primary. As for its definition: it is an accident that does not entail division and non-division in a primary way, i.e. essentially and without mediation, and its meaning is not understood in relation to another. This definition is an incomplete description (rasm naqis) for quality. And it is the ultimate in the highest genera, for due to their simplicity, on the view that they cannot be composed of equal matters, they are not defined at all nor completely described. It is permissible to define them descriptively by existential and non-existential matters, provided that those matters are clearer than what is known by them among the highest genera. So it is not valid to say, for example, 'Substance is what is not an accident', for substance and accident are equal in knowledge and ignorance, so it is not allowed to mention one in the definition of the other. Nor is it valid to say 'Quantity is what is not a quality nor a place nor the rest of the categories', because they are not clearer than quantity to be taken in its definition. So our saying 'accident' includes all accidents. And we guarded by our saying 'does not entail division' against quantity, for it entails division by essence; and by our saying 'does not entail non-division' against unity and point, which entail it according to those who say they are accidents, i.e. according to the view that they exist externally. But according to the view that they are considerations, there is no need for this restriction because they are not included in accident, as was indicated. And by our saying 'primary entailment' we guard against the exclusion of knowledge of a single thing, which is simple. That both are from... His statement: (They are not defined at all, completely or incompletely, because it is necessary to mention the genus in them, and they have no genus.) His statement: (and non-existential) like the mentioned definition. Likewise his statement: (does not entail division) meaning acceptance of hypothetical division, for quantity does not entail actual division since the divider may not divide it. It was mentioned by the author that its acceptivity does not preclude its actuality. His statement: (and by our saying 'primary entailment' etc.) Some said: the commentator followed the author in making 'primary' a restriction for absolute entailment without addressing what it indicates, showing his approval of the necessity of attaching the mentioned restriction to that absolute.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage introduces the third observatory on qualities (al-kayfiyyat) and provides a definition of quality (al-kayf). It begins by explaining why the study of qualities is presented before other categories: because qualities are more sound in existence, as they include sensible things which are the most apparent. However, quantity was presented before quality due to material and abstract considerations. The section then moves to the definition: quality is an accident that does not entail division or non-division in a primary way. This definition is an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because quality belongs to the highest genera, which cannot be fully defined due to their simplicity. The author then discusses conditions for correct definitions: the definiens must be clearer than the definiendum. For example, one cannot define substance as 'what is not an accident' because they are equally known. Similarly, quantity cannot be defined by negating other categories. The definition's components are explained: 'accident' covers all accidents; 'does not entail division' excludes quantity; 'does not entail non-division' excludes unity and point if considered accidents; 'primary entailment' excludes accidental predicates like knowledge of a simple thing. The commentary clarifies that the definition guards against various categories. The passage concludes with a note that highest genera cannot be properly defined and that the condition of clarity must be satisfied.

Key Terms
كيف
عرض
قسمة
لاقسمة
اقتضاء أولي
رسم ناقص
جنس عال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining quality simply as 'an accident that does not entail division or non-division' is sufficient without the condition 'primary entailment'.
Correction: The 'primary entailment' condition is crucial because without it, the definition would accidentally include cases like knowledge of a single simple thing, which is an accident but does entail non-division in a secondary way. The primary condition ensures the definition is precise and excludes such cases.

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of quality called an 'incomplete description' (rasm naqis)?
A) Because it uses negation
B) Because quality is a highest genus and cannot be fully defined
C) Because it is too short
D) Because it does not mention the differentia

Key Takeaway
The definition of quality is an incomplete description that carefully delimits it from other categories by three restrictions, highlighting the precision required in kalam definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition of quality guards against division and non-division. What area of your faith do you need to guard against extremes—either excessive division (e.g., splitting hairs) or neglect (treating matters as indivisible)? How will you commit to balance this week?
?
The author says quality is 'more sound in existence' because it includes sensibles. When you reflect on your own experience of God's creation, do you take the most 'apparent' signs (the sensibles) for granted? What one sensible thing will you meditate on today to connect it to Islamic teachings?
?
Precision in defining terms is shown to be crucial. In what area of your life—prayer, speech, intentions—have you been imprecise? Write one concrete change you will make today to be more exact in your practice.

The Last Condition in the Definition of Quality
Explains the last condition in the definition of quality: not understood relative to others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the last condition in the definition of quality and how it distinguishes qualities from relative accidents.

Core Idea
Qualities are self-contained; they do not depend on others for their meaning.

This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial point in the definition of quality. The definer adds a condition that the meaning of a quality is not understood in relation to another, which excludes relative accidents. But what about qualities like knowledge that require objects? Sanusi explains that although such qualities entail objects, their conception does not depend on them. Let's examine this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Notice the two parts: the condition itself and the clarification about entailment.
2
Identify the last condition
Find the phrase 'its meaning is not understood in relation to another' and understand that it excludes relative accidents (al-a'raaḍ al-nisbiyya). Ask why this is necessary.
3
Understand the nuance
The passage explains that qualities like knowledge entail objects but do not depend on them. Distinguish between 'dependence' (tawaqquf) and 'entailment' (istilzam). The conception of a quality gives rise to its object, not vice versa.
This nuance preserves the definition while accommodating qualities that involve relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولياً، بل بواسطة معلومة والعلم الثاني يقتضي القسمة كذلك فلولا تقييد الاقتضاء بالأولية لخرجا عن الحد مع أنهما من مقولة الكيف ) وبالأخير). أي واحترزنا بالقيد الأخير وهو قولنا : ولا يكون معناه معقولا بالقياس إلى الغير ) عن النسب ) أي الأعراض النسبية، فإنها معقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها . وأما الكيفيات فليست معانيها في أنفسها مقيسة إلى غيرها لما عرفت من أنها لا تقتضي لذاتها النسبة. وقد ذكر بعضهم في موضع القيد الأخير قوله : ولا يتوقف تصوره على تصور غيره فإنَّ الأعراض النسبية تتوقف تصوراتها على تصور أمور أخر، بخلاف الكيفيات فإنها قد يستلزم تصورها تصور غيرها كالإدراك والعلم والقدرة والشهوة والغضب ونظائرها فإنها لا تتصور بدون متعلقاتها. أعني المدرك والمعلوم مثلاً لكن ليس تصوراتها متوقفة على تصورات المتعلقات معلولة لها كما في النسب بل تصوراتها موجبة لتصورات متعلقاتها
First, but through something known, and the second knowledge entails division likewise, so if not for restricting the entailment by firstness, they would exit the definition despite being from the category of quality, and by the last [restriction]. That is, we guarded by the last restriction, which is our saying: 'and its meaning is not understood in relation to another,' from relations, i.e., relative accidents, for they are understood in relation to another. As for qualities, their meanings in themselves are not measured in relation to another, because you know that they do not entail relation by themselves. And some have mentioned in place of the last restriction his saying: 'and its conception does not depend on the conception of another,' for relative accidents' conceptions depend on the conception of other things, unlike qualities, for their conception may entail the conception of something else, like perception, knowledge, power, desire, anger, and their likes, for they are not conceived without their objects. I mean the perceived and the known for example, but their conceptions are not dependent on the conceptions of the objects as effects of them as in the case of relations, rather their conceptions give rise to the conceptions of their objects.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage explains the last condition in the definition of quality (al-kayf). The definer says 'its meaning is not understood in relation to another' to exclude relative accidents (al-a'raaḍ al-nisbiyya), which are only understood in relation to something else. Qualities, by contrast, are not inherently relative; their meanings are not measured against another. Some scholars proposed an alternative phrasing: 'its conception does not depend on the conception of another.' The author then clarifies that even for qualities like knowledge, which require an object (e.g., the known), their conception is not dependent on the object's conception in the way that relative accidents are. For qualities, the conception of the quality gives rise to the conception of its object, not the reverse. Thus, the condition is that the quality does not depend on another, even if it entails another.

Key Terms
كيف
كيفية
أعراض نسبية
توقف
استلزام
اقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qualities that involve an object (like knowledge) are relative because they require an object.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between dependence and entailment: the conception of a quality does not depend on its object; rather it gives rise to the object's conception. Thus, it is not relative.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the last condition 'its meaning is not understood in relation to another'?
A) To include relative accidents
B) To exclude relative accidents
C) To exclude all qualities that involve relation
D) To include qualities that are not relative

Key Takeaway
The last condition in the definition of quality ensures that qualities are not understood as relative, even when they entail objects, because their conception does not depend on those objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own character, are there 'qualities' you claim to have that actually depend on others for their meaning — like being a good father only when your children behave? Reflect on whether your virtues are self-contained or relative.
?
The passage distinguishes between a quality that entails an object (like knowledge) and a quality that depends on an object. Are there aspects of your faith (like your knowledge of Allah) that you treat as dependent on circumstances rather than as self-contained qualities that give rise to those circumstances? What would change if you internalized this distinction?

Refuting the Objection on Composite and Simple Qualities
Refutation of an objection on composite and simple qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that qualities must entail division or non-division is refuted, and how a simple quality like blackness can still be divided by its subject.

Core Idea
Simplicity does not mean indivisibility—only that there are no external parts.

This passage from al-Mawaqif addresses a key objection to the definition of quality. The critic claims that whether a quality is composite or simple, the definition fails. But the answer reveals a subtle distinction between external parts and divisibility by the subject. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the dilemma: if a quality is composite, it entails division; if simple, it entails non-division. Thus the definition (quality's conception does not require division) fails for both.
Note the critic assumes composition and simplicity are mutually exclusive and cover all possibilities.
2
Examine the answer for composite qualities
The answer argues that composition is actually nullified by division, so a composite quality does not entail division. The entailer (composition) and entailed (division) cannot coexist.
This is a logical point: if division occurs, the quality is no longer composite, so composition cannot be the cause of division.
3
Examine the answer for simple qualities
External simplicity means the quality has no external parts, not that it cannot be divided. Blackness is simple externally yet divisible by the division of its subject.
This shows that division can come from the subject, not from the quality itself.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Understand that the definition of quality is about how we conceive it, not about its external divisibility. The objection fails because it confuses external parts with divisibility by subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصوره بدون تصور القسمة وأما ثانياً فلان في الحلول السرياني المحل، والحال متلازمان في الانقسام وعدمه إلى الأجزاء المتباينة في الوضع فالقول : بالتفرقة وهم لا يقال : الكيفيات إما مركبة في الخارج فتكون مقتضية للقسمة أو بسيطة فتكون مقتضية للاقسمة فلا يكون التعريف صادقاً على شيء من أفراد المعرف لأنا نقول : التركيب يبطل بالقسمة فلا يكون مقتضياً لها لأن المقتضى يجامع المقتضي والبساطة الخارجية تقتضي أن لا يكون لها جزء خارجي لا أن لا ينقسم، فإن السواد بسيط في الخارج منقسم بحسب انقسام المحل .
Its conception without conception of division. And secondly, because in the case of substantial inherence the subject and property are inseparable in division and non-division into parts disparate in position, so the claim of separation is an illusion. One cannot say: qualities are either composite in the external world, thus they entail division, or simple, thus they entail non-division, so the definition would not apply to any individuals of the defined. For we say: composition is nullified by division, so it does not entail it, because the entailer coexists with the entailed; and external simplicity requires that it has no external part, not that it cannot be divided. For blackness is simple externally yet divided by the division of its subject.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the definition of quality. The objection argues that qualities are either composite (murakkaba) or simple (basīṭa). If composite, they entail division; if simple, they entail non-division. Since the definition says quality does not entail division (or non-division? Actually, the condition is 'its conception does not require conception of division'), the objection claims the definition fails for both. The answer: for composite qualities, composition is actually nullified by division, so they do not entail division; for simple qualities, external simplicity only means no external parts, not that they cannot be divided. For example, blackness is simple externally but can be divided according to the division of its subject. Thus, the definition holds.

Key Terms

بساطة
انقسام
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple qualities cannot be divided at all.
Correction: External simplicity only means no external parts; division can still occur through the division of the subject, as with blackness.

Checkpoint
How does the answer refute the objection that simple qualities entail non-division?
A) By showing simple qualities are actually composite
B) By arguing external simplicity does not prevent division by subject
C) By changing the definition
D) By denying the existence of simple qualities

Key Takeaway
The definition of quality does not require that a quality be indivisible; it only requires that its conception does not necessitate division, and this holds for both composite and simple qualities when properly understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage shows that careful reasoning resolves apparent contradictions. Think of a time when you encountered a challenge to your faith that seemed like a contradiction. Did you dismiss it, or did you seek to understand the reasoning? Are there any current doubts you are avoiding because they feel too complex? Name them before Allah and commit to studying one with the help of a teacher or reliable text.
?
Blackness is simple yet divided by the subject it inheres in. Consider your own character traits—what appears simple on the outside may be affected by the 'subject' of your circumstances. Are there areas of your spiritual life (e.g., patience, gratitude) that you assume are fixed and simple, but that are actually influenced by your environment? How can you consciously nurture these qualities to remain steadfast regardless of external changes?

The Correct Phrasing of the Condition
Justification of the condition's wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author prefers the phrase 'understood in relation to another' over 'does not depend on the conception of another' for the condition of quality.

Core Idea
The condition’s wording must account for accidental relations, not just essential ones.

In this passage, the commentator justifies the specific wording chosen for the condition of quality. The alternative phrasing might fail to exclude relative accidents if relation is accidental. The author shows why his formulation is precise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage that explains the two possible phrasings and the rationale for choosing one.
2
Identify the problem with the alternative
Notice that if relation is accidental, the conception of the subject does not depend on the accident, so the condition 'does not depend on the conception of another' would not exclude relative accidents.
3
Understand the preferred phrasing
The chosen phrase 'understood in relation to another' captures the logical dependence correctly regardless of whether relation is essential or accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( معقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها لاقتضائها النسبة الموجبة لكونها معقولة بالقياس إلى ما ينسب إليه . قوله : ( لا تقتضي لذاتها النسبة ) وإن كانت عارضة لها . قوله على تصور غيره المراد بالغير الأمر الخارجي كما هو المتبادر فلا يلزم خروج الكيفيات المركبة . قوله : ( فإن الأعراض النسبية الخ هذا على تقدير كون النسبة ذاتياً لها ظاهر وأما على تقدير عروضها لها فلا لأن تصور المعروض لا يتوقف على تصور العارض ولأجل هذا عدل عنه إلى قوله : ولا يكون معناه معقولا بالقياس إلى الغير. قوله : (معلولة لها أشار إلى أنَّ المراد نفي التوقف الذي يقتضي التقدم لا الاستلزام. قوله : ( وكذا الحال ) أي فى أنها موجبة لتصورات متعلقاتها فغير متوقفة عليها . قوله : (بل تصوراتها موجبة لتصورات متعلقاتها في أن حال الأعراض النسبية على المذهب المشهور هو أن النسبة لازمة لها لا ذاتية ولذلك يقال : تصورها يستلزم تصور غيرها ويوجبه وأما التوقف فممنوع . قوله : ( وكالجذرية والكعبية) أعاد الكاف لكونها من العوارض العددية لا المقدارية واعلم فإنها تعقل العلم أولاً ثم تدرك متعلقه وكذا الحال في الكيفيات المخصوصة بالكميات كالاستقامة والانحناء والتثليث والتربيع وكالجذرية والكعبية واعترض عليه بخروج الكيفيات المكتسبة بالحدود والرسوم والجواب : إِنَّ المراد بالتوقف امتناع حصول تصورها بدون الغير لا مجرد الترتب والحصول به والتصورات المكتسبة يمكن حصولها بالبداهة وبرسوم أخرى . أنه إذا ضرب عدد في نفسه فذلك العدد هو الجذر، والحاصل المجذور والمربع أيضاً ثم إذا ضرب ذلك الجذر في ذلك الحاصل فما حصل هو المكعب، فالاثنان جذر الأربعة وكعب الثمانية. قوله : ( واعترض عليه بخروج الكيفيات المكتسبة (الخ قيد بالمكتسبة لظهور النقض بها، وإن كان كل كيفية مركبة كذلك لأن تصور الكل موقوف على تصور الجزء فإن قلت : الأمور النسبية لو كانت مكتسبة متوقفة تصوراتها على تصورات معرفاتها لم تعد نسبية بهذا الاعتبار بل باعتبار أن تعقل ذواتها ضرورية كانت أو مكتسبة بالقياس إلى تعقلات أمور أخر، وهذا المعنى لا يتحقق في الكيفيات المكتسبة. وأيضاً المراد بالغير هو الغير حقيقة والتغاير بين الحد والمحدود اعتباري كما حقق في موضعه :قلت أما الأول فلا يفيد لأن حاصل الاعتراض عدم شمول التعريف بمنطوقه إياه، فكيف يفيد إن عد الأعراض النسبية المكتسبة نسبية بهذا الاعتبار، لا باعتبار كذا اللهم إلا أن يقال: حاصله أن كون نسبيتها بذلك الاعتبار قرينة على أن المراد بالغير في تعريف الكيف الخارج على أنه لا يدفع الاعتراض بالأعراض المكتسبة بالرسوم. اللهم إلا أن يضم أيضاً أنَّ المراد عدم توقف كنه حقائقها . وأما الثاني : فلان الأعراض بالنسبة إلى كل جزء من أجزاء الحد والتغاير حينئذ حقيقي لا بالنسبة إلى مجموع الحد وحمل الغير على اصطلاح المتكلمين لا يلتفت إليه في هذا المقام .
His saying: 'understood in relation to another' because it entails the relation that necessitates being understood in relation to what it is related to. His saying: 'do not entail relation by themselves' even if it is accidental to them. His saying: 'on the conception of another' means 'another' as an external thing as is apparent, so there is no necessary exclusion of composite qualities. His saying: 'for relative accidents' etc. This is apparent on the assumption that relation is essential to them, but on the assumption that it is accidental to them, it is not, because the conception of the subject does not depend on the conception of the accident. For this reason, he turned from it to his saying: 'and its meaning is not understood in relation to another.' His saying: 'as effect of them' indicates that what is meant is the negation of dependence that implies priority, not entailment. His saying: 'and likewise the case' i.e., that they give rise to the conceptions of their objects, so they are not dependent on them. His saying: 'rather their conceptions give rise to the conceptions of their objects' regarding the state of relative accidents on the well-known doctrine that relation is necessary for them not essential, therefore it is said: their conception entails the conception of another and necessitates it, but dependence is prohibited. His saying: 'and like jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya' he repeated the kaf because they are among numerical accidents not quantitative. And know that they conceive knowledge first then perceive its object, and likewise in the case of qualities specific to quantities like straightness, curvature, triangularity, quadrangularity, and like jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya. And an objection was raised about the exclusion of acquired qualities by definitions and diagrams. And the answer: What is meant by dependence is the impossibility of the occurrence of their conception without the other, not mere order and occurrence with it, and acquired conceptions can occur by intuition and by other diagrams. For if a number is multiplied by itself, that number is the root, the result is the squared, also called the square; then if that root is multiplied by that result, what results is the cube, so two is the root of four and the cube of eight. His saying: 'and an objection was raised about the exclusion of acquired qualities' etc. He qualified by 'acquired' because of the apparent refutation by them, even though every composite quality is likewise because the conception of the whole depends on the conception of the part. If you say: relative things, if they are acquired, their conceptions depending on the conceptions of their definers, they would not be relative in this consideration, but rather in consideration that the intellection of their essences, whether necessary or acquired, is relative to intellections of other things, and this meaning is not realized in acquired qualities. Also, what is meant by 'other' is 'other' in reality, and the distinction between the definition and the defined is conceptual, as established in its place. I say: As for the first, it does not benefit because the gist of the objection is the failure of the definition to include them by its wording, so how does it benefit if it counts acquired relative accidents as relative in this consideration, not in that consideration? Unless it is said: its gist is that their being relative in that consideration is evidence that what is meant by 'other' in the definition of quality is external, and that does not repel the objection regarding acquired accidents by diagrams. Unless it is also added that what is meant is the non-dependence of the quiddity of their realities. And as for the second: because accidents in relation to every part of the definition are then truly distinct, not in relation to the entire definition, and applying 'other' in the terminology of the theologians is not heeded in this context.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the last condition of the definition of quality. It first justifies the phrasing 'understood in relation to another' and explains that the alternative phrasing 'does not depend on the conception of another' would exclude relative accidents only if relation is essential to them; if accidental, it would not work, so the author chose the former. It then clarifies that the negation of dependence means impossibility without the other, not mere order. The commentary also discusses the example of jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya (square and cube) as numerical accidents. Then it presents an objection that acquired qualities (e.g., definitions by diagrams) are excluded by the condition because their conception depends on the conception of the defining terms. The answer: what is meant by dependence is strict impossibility without the other, not the fact that they are acquired through definitions. Acquired conceptions can be intuitive. The passage then engages in a dialectic: the objector may argue that relative accidents acquired through definitions are still relative, but the response distinguishes between being relative by essence and being relative by consideration. The final point: the term 'other' in the definition is meant in a real sense, not conceptual, so the distinction between a definition and its parts does not count as 'other' in the relevant sense.

Key Terms
نسبة ذاتية
نسبة عارضة
أعراض نسبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author prefer the phrase 'understood in relation to another' over 'does not depend on the conception of another'?
A) Because it is shorter and more concise.
B) Because the latter would not exclude relative accidents if relation is accidental to them.
C) Because the latter is ambiguous about what 'another' means.
D) Because the former is the original wording from the definition.

Key Takeaway
The condition's wording must be robust whether relation is essential or accidental, and the author's chosen phrase achieves this.

Clarifying Dependence and the Example of Numerical Qualities
Clarification of dependence and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the negation of dependence in the condition means strict impossibility without the other, and you will see how numerical qualities like jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya illustrate relative accidents.

Core Idea
Dependence means impossibility, not mere order; numerical qualities show how conception implies relation.

The commentator now clarifies the precise meaning of 'does not depend' in the condition. It does not mean simple order or entailment but rather the impossibility of conceiving one without the other. He then gives examples of numerical and quantitative qualities to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage that explains the meaning of negation of dependence and the examples.
2
Understand the clarification
Note that 'dependence' here means that conception cannot occur without the other, not just that one follows the other.
3
Study the examples
The qualities like jadhrīyya (square) and ka'bīyya (cube) are relative: you first conceive knowledge, then its object. Similarly for geometric shapes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( معقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها لاقتضائها النسبة الموجبة لكونها معقولة بالقياس إلى ما ينسب إليه . قوله : ( لا تقتضي لذاتها النسبة ) وإن كانت عارضة لها . قوله على تصور غيره المراد بالغير الأمر الخارجي كما هو المتبادر فلا يلزم خروج الكيفيات المركبة . قوله : ( فإن الأعراض النسبية الخ هذا على تقدير كون النسبة ذاتياً لها ظاهر وأما على تقدير عروضها لها فلا لأن تصور المعروض لا يتوقف على تصور العارض ولأجل هذا عدل عنه إلى قوله : ولا يكون معناه معقولا بالقياس إلى الغير. قوله : (معلولة لها أشار إلى أنَّ المراد نفي التوقف الذي يقتضي التقدم لا الاستلزام. قوله : ( وكذا الحال ) أي فى أنها موجبة لتصورات متعلقاتها فغير متوقفة عليها . قوله : (بل تصوراتها موجبة لتصورات متعلقاتها في أن حال الأعراض النسبية على المذهب المشهور هو أن النسبة لازمة لها لا ذاتية ولذلك يقال : تصورها يستلزم تصور غيرها ويوجبه وأما التوقف فممنوع . قوله : ( وكالجذرية والكعبية) أعاد الكاف لكونها من العوارض العددية لا المقدارية واعلم فإنها تعقل العلم أولاً ثم تدرك متعلقه وكذا الحال في الكيفيات المخصوصة بالكميات كالاستقامة والانحناء والتثليث والتربيع وكالجذرية والكعبية واعترض عليه بخروج الكيفيات المكتسبة بالحدود والرسوم والجواب : إِنَّ المراد بالتوقف امتناع حصول تصورها بدون الغير لا مجرد الترتب والحصول به والتصورات المكتسبة يمكن حصولها بالبداهة وبرسوم أخرى . أنه إذا ضرب عدد في نفسه فذلك العدد هو الجذر، والحاصل المجذور والمربع أيضاً ثم إذا ضرب ذلك الجذر في ذلك الحاصل فما حصل هو المكعب، فالاثنان جذر الأربعة وكعب الثمانية. قوله : ( واعترض عليه بخروج الكيفيات المكتسبة (الخ قيد بالمكتسبة لظهور النقض بها، وإن كان كل كيفية مركبة كذلك لأن تصور الكل موقوف على تصور الجزء فإن قلت : الأمور النسبية لو كانت مكتسبة متوقفة تصوراتها على تصورات معرفاتها لم تعد نسبية بهذا الاعتبار بل باعتبار أن تعقل ذواتها ضرورية كانت أو مكتسبة بالقياس إلى تعقلات أمور أخر، وهذا المعنى لا يتحقق في الكيفيات المكتسبة. وأيضاً المراد بالغير هو الغير حقيقة والتغاير بين الحد والمحدود اعتباري كما حقق في موضعه :قلت أما الأول فلا يفيد لأن حاصل الاعتراض عدم شمول التعريف بمنطوقه إياه، فكيف يفيد إن عد الأعراض النسبية المكتسبة نسبية بهذا الاعتبار، لا باعتبار كذا اللهم إلا أن يقال: حاصله أن كون نسبيتها بذلك الاعتبار قرينة على أن المراد بالغير في تعريف الكيف الخارج على أنه لا يدفع الاعتراض بالأعراض المكتسبة بالرسوم. اللهم إلا أن يضم أيضاً أنَّ المراد عدم توقف كنه حقائقها . وأما الثاني : فلان الأعراض بالنسبة إلى كل جزء من أجزاء الحد والتغاير حينئذ حقيقي لا بالنسبة إلى مجموع الحد وحمل الغير على اصطلاح المتكلمين لا يلتفت إليه في هذا المقام .
His saying: 'understood in relation to another' because it entails the relation that necessitates being understood in relation to what it is related to. His saying: 'do not entail relation by themselves' even if it is accidental to them. His saying: 'on the conception of another' means 'another' as an external thing as is apparent, so there is no necessary exclusion of composite qualities. His saying: 'for relative accidents' etc. This is apparent on the assumption that relation is essential to them, but on the assumption that it is accidental to them, it is not, because the conception of the subject does not depend on the conception of the accident. For this reason, he turned from it to his saying: 'and its meaning is not understood in relation to another.' His saying: 'as effect of them' indicates that what is meant is the negation of dependence that implies priority, not entailment. His saying: 'and likewise the case' i.e., that they give rise to the conceptions of their objects, so they are not dependent on them. His saying: 'rather their conceptions give rise to the conceptions of their objects' regarding the state of relative accidents on the well-known doctrine that relation is necessary for them not essential, therefore it is said: their conception entails the conception of another and necessitates it, but dependence is prohibited. His saying: 'and like jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya' he repeated the kaf because they are among numerical accidents not quantitative. And know that they conceive knowledge first then perceive its object, and likewise in the case of qualities specific to quantities like straightness, curvature, triangularity, quadrangularity, and like jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya. And an objection was raised about the exclusion of acquired qualities by definitions and diagrams. And the answer: What is meant by dependence is the impossibility of the occurrence of their conception without the other, not mere order and occurrence with it, and acquired conceptions can occur by intuition and by other diagrams. For if a number is multiplied by itself, that number is the root, the result is the squared, also called the square; then if that root is multiplied by that result, what results is the cube, so two is the root of four and the cube of eight. His saying: 'and an objection was raised about the exclusion of acquired qualities' etc. He qualified by 'acquired' because of the apparent refutation by them, even though every composite quality is likewise because the conception of the whole depends on the conception of the part. If you say: relative things, if they are acquired, their conceptions depending on the conceptions of their definers, they would not be relative in this consideration, but rather in consideration that the intellection of their essences, whether necessary or acquired, is relative to intellections of other things, and this meaning is not realized in acquired qualities. Also, what is meant by 'other' is 'other' in reality, and the distinction between the definition and the defined is conceptual, as established in its place. I say: As for the first, it does not benefit because the gist of the objection is the failure of the definition to include them by its wording, so how does it benefit if it counts acquired relative accidents as relative in this consideration, not in that consideration? Unless it is said: its gist is that their being relative in that consideration is evidence that what is meant by 'other' in the definition of quality is external, and that does not repel the objection regarding acquired accidents by diagrams. Unless it is also added that what is meant is the non-dependence of the quiddity of their realities. And as for the second: because accidents in relation to every part of the definition are then truly distinct, not in relation to the entire definition, and applying 'other' in the terminology of the theologians is not heeded in this context.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the last condition of the definition of quality. It first justifies the phrasing 'understood in relation to another' and explains that the alternative phrasing 'does not depend on the conception of another' would exclude relative accidents only if relation is essential to them; if accidental, it would not work, so the author chose the former. It then clarifies that the negation of dependence means impossibility without the other, not mere order. The commentary also discusses the example of jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya (square and cube) as numerical accidents. Then it presents an objection that acquired qualities (e.g., definitions by diagrams) are excluded by the condition because their conception depends on the conception of the defining terms. The answer: what is meant by dependence is strict impossibility without the other, not the fact that they are acquired through definitions. Acquired conceptions can be intuitive. The passage then engages in a dialectic: the objector may argue that relative accidents acquired through definitions are still relative, but the response distinguishes between being relative by essence and being relative by consideration. The final point: the term 'other' in the definition is meant in a real sense, not conceptual, so the distinction between a definition and its parts does not count as 'other' in the relevant sense.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator mean by 'negation of dependence that implies priority'?
A) Dependence means the one cannot exist without the other.
B) Dependence means conception cannot occur without the other.
C) Dependence means one follows the other in time.
D) Dependence means one is the cause of the other.

Key Takeaway
Dependence in the condition means strict impossibility of conception without the other, not just logical entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to define a central concept of your faith—like ‘iman’ or ‘tawakkul’—have you ever confused necessary entailment with dependence? How might that error have affected the way you live out that concept? This week, pick one concept and consciously examine whether your understanding of it depends on something else or merely entails it.
?
The passage says that relative accidents have relation as necessary but not essential. In your own life, what relationships or roles do you treat as essential to your identity that are actually only necessary? What would change if you recognized the difference? Commit to letting go of one such false essential attachment this month.

Objection of Acquired Qualities and the Answer
Objection of acquired qualities and its resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that acquired qualities (like those from definitions) might violate the condition, and how the commentator answers by distinguishing between strict dependence and ordinary acquisition.

Core Idea
Acquired qualities do not break the condition because dependence is impossibility, not mere acquisition.

The commentator now addresses a potential objection: some qualities are acquired through definitions or diagrams, so their conception seems to depend on something else. This would seem to exclude them from being relative accidents. The answer carefully distinguishes between strict impossibility of conception without the other and the fact that they can be acquired through definitions or even intuitively.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the objection and the answer, plus the extended dialectic that follows.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says that acquired qualities (e.g., definitions by diagrams) are excluded because their conception depends on the definition.
3
Follow the answer
The commentator says that dependence here means impossibility without the other, not mere order; acquired conceptions can be intuitive and do not require the definition.
4
Examine the dialectic
The objector tries to argue that relative accidents acquired are still relative, but the commentator distinguishes between real and conceptual other.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the last condition of the definition of quality. It first justifies the phrasing 'understood in relation to another' and explains that the alternative phrasing 'does not depend on the conception of another' would exclude relative accidents only if relation is essential to them; if accidental, it would not work, so the author chose the former. It then clarifies that the negation of dependence means impossibility without the other, not mere order. The commentary also discusses the example of jadhrīyya and ka'bīyya (square and cube) as numerical accidents. Then it presents an objection that acquired qualities (e.g., definitions by diagrams) are excluded by the condition because their conception depends on the conception of the defining terms. The answer: what is meant by dependence is strict impossibility without the other, not the fact that they are acquired through definitions. Acquired conceptions can be intuitive. The passage then engages in a dialectic: the objector may argue that relative accidents acquired through definitions are still relative, but the response distinguishes between being relative by essence and being relative by consideration. The final point: the term 'other' in the definition is meant in a real sense, not conceptual, so the distinction between a definition and its parts does not count as 'other' in the relevant sense.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acquired qualities cannot be relative because their conception depends on definitions.
Correction: Dependence in the condition means strict impossibility without the other; acquired conceptions can be intuitive and do not require the definition.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the objection about acquired qualities?
A) Acquired qualities are not actually relative.
B) Dependence means impossibility, not mere order; acquired conceptions can be intuitive.
C) The condition only applies to essential qualities.
D) Acquired qualities are excluded from the definition.

Key Takeaway
Acquired qualities do not violate the condition because dependence is strict impossibility, and they can be conceived intuitively.

The Four Divisions of Quality and Their Proof
Enumerates four kinds of quality and gives the first proof by disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four kinds of quality and the logical proof that establishes them through exhaustive disjunction.

Core Idea
Quality is divided into four kinds, proven by exhaustive disjunction.

In this passage, Sanusi enumerates the four types of quality and provides a rigorous proof. He begins by listing them, then shows how induction leads to this classification, but he also offers a logical disjunction that demonstrates necessity. This lesson unpacks both the list and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: first enumeration, then the proof.
The proof uses a chain of dichotomies.
2
Identify the four kinds of quality
List the four kinds: sensible, psychical, specific to quantities, and dispositional. Understand what each refers to.
Sensible includes colors, tastes; psychical includes knowledge, anger; etc.
3
Understand the proof by disjunction
Follow the logical chain: quality is either specific to quantity or not; if not, then either sensible or not; if not sensible, then disposition or perfection (psychical).
This chain covers all possibilities.
4
Recognize the exhaustiveness
Notice that each branch covers all possibilities, leaving no room for missing categories. This is why the proof is strong.
The proof is based on the principle of excluded middle.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why does this classification matter for theology? It helps in analyzing attributes of Allah and His creation.
This foundation is used later in discussing divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أقسامه فهي أربعة) الكيفيات ( المحسوسة و) الكيفيات ( النفسانية و الكيفيات ( المختصة بالكميات والاستعدادات ) أي الكيفيات الاستعدادية ) ومأخذ الحصر) في هذه الأربعة ( هو الاستقراء ) والتتبع. ( ومنهم من أراد إثباته بالترديد بين النفي والإثبات فذكر وجوهاً) أربعة . (الأول) وهو أجودها ( أنه ( أي الكيف (إما أن يختص بالكم أو لا يختص به (وهذا) الذي لا يختص بالكم (إما محسوس) بإحدى الحواس الظاهرة ) أو لا وهذا الذي ليس محسوساً بها، إما استعداد نحو الكمال أو كمال وهذا الأخير هو الكيفيات النفسانية .
And as for its divisions, they are four: [1] sensible qualities, [2] psychical qualities, [3] qualities specific to quantities, and [4] dispositional qualities, i.e., dispositional qualities. And the basis of the enumeration of these four is induction and investigation. And among them is one who wanted to prove it by disjunction between negation and affirmation, and he mentioned four ways. The first, which is the best, is that quality is either specific to quantity or not specific to quantity; and that which is not specific to quantity is either sensible by one of the external senses or not; and that which is not sensible is either a disposition towards perfection or perfection itself; and this last is psychical qualities.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the division of quality (al-kayf) into four kinds. It first lists them: sensible qualities (like colors, tastes), psychical qualities (like knowledge, anger), qualities specific to quantities (like straightness, triangularity), and dispositional qualities (like hardness, softness). It states that the enumeration is based on induction (istiqrā'). Then it describes a method of proof by disjunction (al-tardīd bayna al-nafy wa al-ithbāt). The first and best way: quality is either specific to quantity or not. Those not specific to quantity are either sensible or not sensible. Those not sensible are either a disposition towards perfection or perfection itself. The last category is identified as psychical qualities. This disjunction covers all possibilities.

Key Terms

Correction: The passage shows that the division is logically necessary through exhaustive disjunction, not just empirical observation.

The Four Kinds of Quality and Their Basis in Induction
Enumerates the four kinds of quality and states the basis is induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of quality according to this text and the method of induction used to derive them.

Core Idea
The four qualities are known by induction, not deduction.

In this passage, the author enumerates the types of quality (الكيف). He lists four kinds and explicitly states that this division is obtained through induction and investigation. Understanding this helps distinguish between empirical classification and purely logical division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the four categories listed.
The passage is short but packed.
2
Identify each quality type
List the four qualities: sensible, psychic, specific to quantities, and dispositional. Understand each type.
Sensible qualities are perceived by senses; psychic qualities are states of the soul; qualities specific to quantities include shapes and numbers; dispositional are potentialities.
3
Understand the basis of enumeration
The text says the enumeration is based on induction (الاستقراء) and investigation (التتبع). Reflect on why this is important.
Induction means observation of instances, not a priori reasoning.
4
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: Could these divisions be derived purely by logic? The text says no—they come from empirical investigation.
This is a key point in Islamic epistemology.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the divisions of quality (الكيف) into four types: (1) sensible qualities (المحسوسة), which are perceived by the senses; (2) psychic qualities (النفسانية), which are states of the soul; (3) qualities specific to quantities (المختصة بالكميات), such as geometric shapes and numerical properties; and (4) dispositional qualities (الاستعدادية), which are potentialities. The passage states that the method of arriving at this enumeration is induction (الاستقراء) and empirical investigation (التتبع), meaning that these divisions are based on observing instances of quality, not on a deductive proof.

Definition of Quality and the Objection about Acquired Qualities
Defines quality and defends against an objection about acquired qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of quality (الكيف) in kalam and how it defends against the objection that it excludes qualities acquired through definitions.

Core Idea
Quality is defined by dependence on prior knowledge; acquired qualities are not truly dependent.

In this passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif, the author defines the category of quality (الكيف) and addresses a subtle objection: that the definition fails to include qualities that are acquired through definitions and descriptions. The discussion clarifies what 'dependence' means in kalam terminology and defends the definition by distinguishing between necessary dependence and mere ordering.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Start by reading the passage where quality is defined and examples like straightness, curvature, radicability, and cubicity are given. Notice how the definition links quality to knowledge.
The examples for quantities help illustrate the definition.
2
Identify the objection
Look for the objection that the definition excludes acquired qualities (الكيفيات المكتسبة بالحدود والرسوم). Understand what 'acquired qualities' means in this context.
The objection is introduced with 'واعترض عليه'.
3
Understand the answer about 'dependence'
The answer explains that 'dependence' means impossibility of conception without the other, not mere sequence or attainment. Acquired qualities can be conceived intuitively or via other descriptions.
This distinction is key to resolving the objection.
4
Follow the further clarification
Read the subsequent discussion about relative matters and the nature of 'other' in the definition. The argument refines the definition's scope.
Pay attention to the difference between real and conceptual otherness.
5
Summarize the resolution
Conclude by summarizing how the definition remains valid: it only excludes qualities that cannot be conceived without something else, which acquired qualities are not.
This reinforces the core idea of the lesson.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في تعريفه وأقسامه فإنها تعقل العلم أولاً ثم تدرك متعلقه وكذا الحال في الكيفيات المخصوصة بالكميات كالاستقامة والانحناء والتثليث والتربيع وكالجذرية والكعبية واعترض عليه بخروج الكيفيات المكتسبة بالحدود والرسوم قوله: ( وكالجذرية والكعبية العدد المضروب في نفسه يسمى جذراً والحاصل منه مجذوراً ، وإذا ضرب ذلك العدد في الحاصل من ضرب نفسه كعباً والحاصل مكعباً. يسمى قوله : ( واعترض عليه الخ والجواب : إِنَّ المراد بالتوقف امتناع حصول تصورها بدون الغير لا مجرد الترتب والحصول به والتصورات المكتسبة يمكن حصولها بالبداهة وبرسوم أخرى . أنه إذا ضرب عدد في نفسه فذلك العدد هو الجذر، والحاصل المجذور والمربع أيضاً ثم إذا ضرب ذلك الجذر في ذلك الحاصل فما حصل هو المكعب، فالاثنان جذر الأربعة وكعب الثمانية. قوله : ( واعترض عليه بخروج الكيفيات المكتسبة (الخ قيد بالمكتسبة لظهور النقض بها، وإن كان كل كيفية مركبة كذلك لأن تصور الكل موقوف على تصور الجزء فإن قلت : الأمور النسبية لو كانت مكتسبة متوقفة تصوراتها على تصورات معرفاتها لم تعد نسبية بهذا الاعتبار بل باعتبار أن تعقل ذواتها ضرورية كانت أو مكتسبة بالقياس إلى تعقلات أمور أخر، وهذا المعنى لا يتحقق في الكيفيات المكتسبة. وأيضاً المراد بالغير هو الغير حقيقة والتغاير بين الحد والمحدود اعتباري كما حقق في موضعه :قلت أما الأول فلا يفيد لأن حاصل الاعتراض عدم شمول التعريف بمنطوقه إياه، فكيف يفيد إن عد الأعراض النسبية المكتسبة نسبية بهذا الاعتبار، لا باعتبار كذا اللهم إلا أن يقال: حاصله أن كون نسبيتها بذلك الاعتبار قرينة على أن المراد بالغير في تعريف الكيف الخارج على أنه لا يدفع الاعتراض بالأعراض المكتسبة بالرسوم. اللهم إلا أن يضم أيضاً أنَّ المراد عدم توقف كنه حقائقها . وأما الثاني : فلان الأعراض بالنسبة إلى كل جزء من أجزاء الحد والتغاير حينئذ حقيقي لا بالنسبة إلى مجموع الحد وحمل الغير على اصطلاح المتكلمين لا يلتفت إليه في هذا المقام .
The third observatory - the first objective: concerning its definition and its divisions. For they (qualities) are conceptualized by knowledge first, then their object is perceived, and similarly for qualities specific to quantities, such as straightness, curvature, triangulation, quadrature, and radicability and cubicity. And it was objected upon (the definition) that it excludes qualities acquired by definitions and descriptions. His statement: (And radicability and cubicity: a number multiplied by itself is called a root, and the result is a square; and if that number is multiplied by the result of its self-multiplication, it is a cube, and the result is a cube). He said: (And it was objected upon, etc. The answer: indeed what is meant by dependence is the impossibility of obtaining their conceptualization without the other, not mere ordering and attainment by it; and acquired conceptualizations can be obtained by intuition and by other descriptions. That if a number is multiplied by itself, that number is the root, and the result is both the square and the square; then if that root is multiplied by that result, what results is the cube; so two is the root of four and the cube of eight. His statement: (And it was objected upon regarding the exclusion of acquired qualities, etc.) he restricted it to acquired ones because the refutation is apparent by them, although every composite quality is like that, because the conceptualization of the whole depends on the conceptualization of the part. For if you say: relative matters, if they are acquired and their conceptualizations depend on the conceptualizations of their definients, they are no longer relative in this respect, but rather in respect that the conceptualization of their essences—whether necessary or acquired—is relative to the conceptualizations of other matters, and this meaning is not realized in acquired qualities. Also, what is meant by 'other' is truly other, and the difference between the definition and the defined is conceptual, as verified in its place. I say: As for the first, it is not beneficial, because the gist of the objection is that the definition does not include it by its wording; how then does it benefit if the acquired relative accidents are considered relative in this respect, not in that respect? Unless it is said: its gist is that their being relative in that respect is evidence that what is meant by 'other' in the definition of quality excludes, and that it does not repel the objection by the acquired accidents through descriptions. Unless it is also included that what is meant is the non-dependence of the essence of their realities. And as for the second: because accidents, relative to each part of the parts of the definition, the difference is then real, not relative to the whole of the definition, and applying 'other' to the terminology of the theologians is not regarded in this context.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the category of quality (الكيف). It begins by stating the third observatory and first objective: the definition and divisions of quality. The definition given is that qualities are conceptualized first through knowledge and then their object is perceived; this is also true for qualities specific to quantities, giving examples like straightness, curvature, triangulation, quadrature, radicability, and cubicity. The commentator then explains the terms 'radicability' and 'cubicity' with mathematical examples. An objection is raised that this definition excludes qualities acquired through definitions and descriptions (الكيفيات المكتسبة بالحدود والرسوم). The answer explains that the dependence meant in the definition is the impossibility of conceptualization without the other, not merely being subsequent or attained by it. Acquired conceptualizations can be obtained intuitively or through other descriptions, so they are not truly dependent. The commentator further refines the objection, arguing that even if relative matters depend on definients, they are relative in a different sense, and the definition's 'other' refers to a real other, while the difference between definition and defined is conceptual. The conclusion is that the objection does not invalidate the definition.

Correction: Dependence means impossibility of obtaining the conceptualization without the other; acquired qualities can be conceived without their definients via intuition or other descriptions.

Ibn Sina's Definition of Sensible Quality by Resemblance
Ibn Sina defines sensible quality by resemblance and excludes heaviness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's criterion for sensible qualities—acting by resemblance—and why heaviness initially fails that test.

Core Idea
A quality is sensible only if it produces its likeness in another subject.

Ibn Sina offers a precise definition of sensible quality: it must 'act by resemblance,' meaning it imparts its likeness to something else. Heat makes other things hot; blackness casts its image. Heaviness, however, only moves bodies, not producing heaviness in others. This lesson unpacks that definition and its immediate implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Ibn Sina's definition.
2
Identify the criterion
Note that 'acting by resemblance' is the key condition.
3
Contrast the examples
How do heat and blackness meet the criterion, while heaviness does not?
4
Note al-Razi's commentary
Al-Razi confirms the exclusion of heaviness and lightness.
5
Reflect on the implication
Understand why this classification matters for Kalam.
This connects to the broader atomization of categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من وجوه الحصر ) وقال ابن سينا : ( في الشفاء الكيف (إن فعل بالتشبيه) أي إِنَّ صدر عنه ما يشبهه (فمحسوس) كالحرارة، فإنها تجعل ما يجاور محلها حاراً، وكالسواد فإنه يلقي شبحه في العين وهو مثاله : بخلاف الثقل فإن فعله في الجسم هو التحريك، وليس ثقلا قال الإمام الرازي: هذا تصريح من ابن سينا بإخراج الثقل والخفة عن نوع الكيفيات المحسوسة. ثم إنه عند شروعه في الكيفيات المحسوسة نص على أن الثقل والخفة منها إذ لا يجوز إدخالهما في الكم ولا في مقولة أخرى سوى الكيف، ولا يمكن إدخالهما أيضاً في الأنواع الثلاثة الأخرى من هذه قوله : ( فإن فعله في الجسم أي جسمه كذا في الشفاء . قوله : (هو التحريك ) وأما مدافعة ما يجاوره بتحريك جسمه وإن كان فعله بالواسطة لكنه ليس يثقل إذ هو عبارة عن المدافعة الطبيعية. كذا قيل : وفيه أنَّ الحرارة في المجاور أيضاً كذلك والصواب أن يقال : لم يصدر عن الثقل المدافعة بلا واسطة بل بواسطة التحريك، والمراد أن يكون فعله التشبيه بلا واسطة . قوله : ( إذ لا يجوز إدخالهما في الكم إلخ) في الشفاء يظن بهما أنهما من باب الكمية. قوله: ( ولا يمكن إدخالهما إلخ) في الشفاء قد يظن بهما أنهما من باب القوة واللاقوة. قوله : ( الكيف إن فعل بالتشبيه (الخ قيل: إن أراد الحصر فلا يستقيم، لأن الحرارة تفعل التفريق أيضاً، وإن أراد الاطلاق، فالثقل يفعل شبهه في الحس المشترك عند الحس، كما يلقي السواد شبحه في العين. وأجيب : بأن تأدي الثقل إلى الحس المشترك فرع كونه محسوساً بالحس الظاهر، وذلك أول المسألة فتأمل . قوله : ( بخلاف الثقل (الخ من إخراج الثقل يفهم إخراج الخفة، ولهذا قال الإمام : وهذا تصريح من الشيخ بإخراج الثقل والخفة فإن قلت : الخفة مثلا إما مدافعة صاعدة أو مبدؤها وأياً ما كان فقد يعطي الجسم الملاقي لمحلها مدافعة أو مبدءها كاليد الموضوع على الزق المنفوخ فيه المسكن تحت الماء. قلت : الخفة المدافعة أو مبدأها الطبيعيان ولا يعطي الجسم الملاقي إياهما .
(The second of the ways of enumeration.) And Ibn Sina said: (In al-Shifa: quality, if it acts by resemblance—meaning that what resembles it originates from it—then it is sensible, like heat, for it makes what is adjacent to its location hot; and like blackness, for it casts its image in the eye, and that is its example. Unlike heaviness, for its action in the body is movement, and it is not heaviness. Imam al-Razi said: This is an explicit statement from Ibn Sina excluding heaviness and lightness from the type of sensible qualities. Then, when he began discussing sensible qualities, he stated that heaviness and lightness are among them, for it is not permissible to include them in quantity nor in any other category besides quality, and it is not possible to include them in the other three types either. His statement: (for its action in the body) i.e., its own body, as in al-Shifa. His statement: (is movement) and as for repelling what is adjacent by moving its body, even if it is an action by mediation, it is not heaviness, for it is an expression of natural repulsion. It was said thus, and there is in it that heat in the adjacent is also like that. The correct thing is to say: heaviness does not produce repulsion without mediation, but rather by mediation of movement, and the intention is that its action be resemblance without mediation. His statement: (for it is not permissible to include them in quantity, etc.) in al-Shifa: it is thought that they belong to the category of quantity. His statement: (and it is not possible to include them, etc.) in al-Shifa: it is sometimes thought that they belong to the category of power and lack of power. His statement: (quality, if it acts by resemblance, etc.) It was said: if he intends enumeration, it is not sound, because heat also causes separation, and if he intends absolute description, then heaviness produces its resemblance in the common sense when sensed, just as blackness casts its image in the eye. And it was answered: that the conveyance of heaviness to the common sense is contingent upon its being sensible by the external sense, and that is the beginning of the issue, so reflect. His statement: (unlike heaviness, etc.) from the exclusion of heaviness, the exclusion of lightness is understood, and for this the Imam said: this is an explicit statement of the Sheikh excluding heaviness and lightness. For if you say: lightness, for instance, is either an upward repulsion or its origin, and whichever it is, it may give the body adjacent to its location repulsion or its origin, like the hand placed on a bladder filled with air that is submerged under water. I say: lightness is the natural repulsion or its origin, and it does not give that to the adjacent body.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the second method of enumerating the divisions of quality, attributed to Ibn Sina. Ibn Sina defines a sensible quality as one that acts by resemblance (فعل بالتشبيه), i.e., it produces something similar to itself in another subject. Examples: heat makes neighboring objects hot; blackness casts its image in the eye. Heaviness (الثقل) is excluded because its action is merely movement, not producing heaviness in another body. Imam al-Razi notes that Ibn Sina explicitly excludes heaviness and lightness from sensible qualities. However, when Ibn Sina later lists sensible qualities, he includes heaviness and lightness, because they cannot be placed in other categories. The passage then discusses objections: heat also causes separation, so the definition is not exclusive; heaviness might produce a resemblance in the common sense. The answer is that producing an effect in the common sense presupposes being sensed by the external sense, which is the very point in question. The discussion also clarifies that heaviness and lightness are not actions of producing resemblance directly. The passage ends with a distinction: lightness is a natural repulsion or its origin and does not impart that to an adjacent body. Overall, this lesson explores the attempt to define sensible quality by resemblance and the problematic cases of heaviness and lightness.

Key Terms
التشبيه
المحسوس
الثقل
الخفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think heaviness is sensible because we perceive weight.
Correction: Ibn Sina's criterion is not mere sense perception; it is the production of likeness. Heaviness does not produce heaviness in another body.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why is heaviness not a sensible quality?
A) Because it is not perceived by any sense.
B) Because its action (movement) does not produce a likeness of heaviness in another body.
C) Because it belongs to the category of quantity.
D) Because it is a spiritual quality.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's test for sensible quality is whether it produces its likeness in another subject.

?

The Status of Heaviness and Lightness as Sensible Qualities
Discussion of objections and resolutions regarding heaviness and lightness as sensible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina includes heaviness and lightness among sensible qualities despite his initial exclusion, and how he handles objections.

Core Idea
Heaviness and lightness are sensible by inclusion, not by the resemblance criterion alone.

After excluding heaviness and lightness from sensible qualities by the resemblance test, Ibn Sina later includes them because they cannot be placed elsewhere. This leads to objections: heat also causes separation, and heaviness might produce resemblance in the common sense. The responses clarify that the intended criterion is direct without mediation, and that common sense perception presupposes external sensation. This lesson navigates these subtleties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the argument.
2
Understand the inclusion
Ibn Sina includes heaviness and lightness because they have no other category.
3
Analyze the objections
Two objections: heat also acts differently, and heaviness might produce likeness in the common sense.
4
Examine the responses
The responses clarify that direct resemblance without mediation is key, and that common sense perception depends on external sense.
5
Final point on lightness
Lightness is also excluded because it does not impart its nature to adjacent bodies.

( الثاني ) من وجوه الحصر ) وقال ابن سينا : ( في الشفاء الكيف (إن فعل بالتشبيه) أي إِنَّ صدر عنه ما يشبهه (فمحسوس) كالحرارة، فإنها تجعل ما يجاور محلها حاراً، وكالسواد فإنه يلقي شبحه في العين وهو مثاله : بخلاف الثقل فإن فعله في الجسم هو التحريك، وليس ثقلا قال الإمام الرازي: هذا تصريح من ابن سينا بإخراج الثقل والخفة عن نوع الكيفيات المحسوسة. ثم إنه عند شروعه في الكيفيات المحسوسة نص على أن الثقل والخفة منها إذ لا يجوز إدخالهما في الكم ولا في مقولة أخرى سوى الكيف، ولا يمكن إدخالهما أيضاً في الأنواع الثلاثة الأخرى من هذه قوله : ( فإن فعله في الجسم أي جسمه كذا في الشفاء . قوله : (هو التحريك ) وأما مدافعة ما يجاوره بتحريك جسمه وإن كان فعله بالواسطة لكنه ليس يثقل إذ هو عبارة عن المدافعة الطبيعية. كذا قيل : وفيه أنَّ الحرارة في المجاور أيضاً كذلك والصواب أن يقال : لم يصدر عن الثقل المدافعة بلا واسطة بل بواسطة التحريك، والمراد أن يكون فعله التشبيه بلا واسطة . قوله : ( إذ لا يجوز إدخالهما في الكم إلخ) في الشفاء يظن بهما أنهما من باب الكمية. قوله: ( ولا يمكن إدخالهما إلخ) في الشفاء قد يظن بهما أنهما من باب القوة واللاقوة. قوله : ( الكيف إن فعل بالتشبيه (الخ قيل: إن أراد الحصر فلا يستقيم، لأن الحرارة تفعل التفريق أيضاً، وإن أراد الاطلاق، فالثقل يفعل شبهه في الحس المشترك عند الحس، كما يلقي السواد شبحه في العين. وأجيب : بأن تأدي الثقل إلى الحس المشترك فرع كونه محسوساً بالحس الظاهر، وذلك أول المسألة فتأمل . قوله : ( بخلاف الثقل (الخ من إخراج الثقل يفهم إخراج الخفة، ولهذا قال الإمام : وهذا تصريح من الشيخ بإخراج الثقل والخفة فإن قلت : الخفة مثلا إما مدافعة صاعدة أو مبدؤها وأياً ما كان فقد يعطي الجسم الملاقي لمحلها مدافعة أو مبدءها كاليد الموضوع على الزق المنفوخ فيه المسكن تحت الماء. قلت : الخفة المدافعة أو مبدأها الطبيعيان ولا يعطي الجسم الملاقي إياهما .
(The second of the ways of enumeration.) And Ibn Sina said: (In al-Shifa: quality, if it acts by resemblance—meaning that what resembles it originates from it—then it is sensible, like heat, for it makes what is adjacent to its location hot; and like blackness, for it casts its image in the eye, and that is its example. Unlike heaviness, for its action in the body is movement, and it is not heaviness. Imam al-Razi said: This is an explicit statement from Ibn Sina excluding heaviness and lightness from the type of sensible qualities. Then, when he began discussing sensible qualities, he stated that heaviness and lightness are among them, for it is not permissible to include them in quantity nor in any other category besides quality, and it is not possible to include them in the other three types either. His statement: (for its action in the body) i.e., its own body, as in al-Shifa. His statement: (is movement) and as for repelling what is adjacent by moving its body, even if it is an action by mediation, it is not heaviness, for it is an expression of natural repulsion. It was said thus, and there is in it that heat in the adjacent is also like that. The correct thing is to say: heaviness does not produce repulsion without mediation, but rather by mediation of movement, and the intention is that its action be resemblance without mediation. His statement: (for it is not permissible to include them in quantity, etc.) in al-Shifa: it is thought that they belong to the category of quantity. His statement: (and it is not possible to include them, etc.) in al-Shifa: it is sometimes thought that they belong to the category of power and lack of power. His statement: (quality, if it acts by resemblance, etc.) It was said: if he intends enumeration, it is not sound, because heat also causes separation, and if he intends absolute description, then heaviness produces its resemblance in the common sense when sensed, just as blackness casts its image in the eye. And it was answered: that the conveyance of heaviness to the common sense is contingent upon its being sensible by the external sense, and that is the beginning of the issue, so reflect. His statement: (unlike heaviness, etc.) from the exclusion of heaviness, the exclusion of lightness is understood, and for this the Imam said: this is an explicit statement of the Sheikh excluding heaviness and lightness. For if you say: lightness, for instance, is either an upward repulsion or its origin, and whichever it is, it may give the body adjacent to its location repulsion or its origin, like the hand placed on a bladder filled with air that is submerged under water. I say: lightness is the natural repulsion or its origin, and it does not give that to the adjacent body.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the second method of enumerating the divisions of quality, attributed to Ibn Sina. Ibn Sina defines a sensible quality as one that acts by resemblance (فعل بالتشبيه), i.e., it produces something similar to itself in another subject. Examples: heat makes neighboring objects hot; blackness casts its image in the eye. Heaviness (الثقل) is excluded because its action is merely movement, not producing heaviness in another body. Imam al-Razi notes that Ibn Sina explicitly excludes heaviness and lightness from sensible qualities. However, when Ibn Sina later lists sensible qualities, he includes heaviness and lightness, because they cannot be placed in other categories. The passage then discusses objections: heat also causes separation, so the definition is not exclusive; heaviness might produce a resemblance in the common sense. The answer is that producing an effect in the common sense presupposes being sensed by the external sense, which is the very point in question. The discussion also clarifies that heaviness and lightness are not actions of producing resemblance directly. The passage ends with a distinction: lightness is a natural repulsion or its origin and does not impart that to an adjacent body. Overall, this lesson explores the attempt to define sensible quality by resemblance and the problematic cases of heaviness and lightness.

Misconception: One might think heaviness and lightness are not sensible because they don't produce resemblance.
Correction: Ibn Sina includes them because they cannot be classified elsewhere, and the responses address objections about similarity and common sense.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina include heaviness and lightness among sensible qualities despite excluding them by the resemblance criterion?
A) Because they do produce resemblance in the common sense.
B) Because they cannot be placed in any other category (quantity, etc.).
C) Because their action is movement, which is a form of resemblance.
D) Because he changed his mind.

Key Takeaway
Heaviness and lightness are sensible qualities by categorical necessity, and the objections are resolved by clarifying directness and sense perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality in your own character that you consider 'inactive' or 'heavy'—something that doesn't seem to produce immediate, noticeable effects in your life. How might you be dismissing its reality simply because it doesn't 'look' like other virtues? What would it take for you to acknowledge its weight and begin working with it?
?
The passage shows that even an apparent lack of resemblance doesn't disqualify something from being real. In your spiritual life, what 'heavy' realities (like patience, endurance, or silence) have you ignored because they weren't 'sweet' or 'light'? How can you start perceiving them as genuine qualities that affect your soul?
?
Ibn Sina's argument forces us to reconsider what we count as real based on direct perception. When have you judged a person, situation, or even a spiritual state as 'not affecting you' because you didn't feel it immediately? Reflect on a time you were wrong about that—what did you miss, and how can you avoid that blind spot now?

Classification of Sensible Qualities into Stable and Unstable
Al-Isfahani defines sensible qualities and divides them into stable (infi'aliyyat) and unstable (infi'alat).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Isfahani defines sensible qualities and why they are divided into infi'aliyyat and infi'alat.

Core Idea
Stable sensible qualities cause sensation and depend on temperament.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif begins the discussion of sensible qualities, the most apparent category of quality. Al-Isfahani not only defines them but also establishes a crucial division based on stability, which determines the technical terms used. Understanding this division is essential for grasping the subsequent analysis of qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parenthetical insertions that clarify the text.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides sensible qualities into two types based on stability. Find the terms 'infi'aliyyat' and 'infi'alat' and note their definitions.
3
Understand the two reasons for naming
The passage gives two reasons why stable qualities are called infi'aliyyat. List them and consider why each is relevant.
These reasons connect to the broader kalam concept of affection (infi'al).
4
Examine the case of unstable qualities
Notice that unstable qualities are also called infi'alat despite sharing the same two aspects. Understand why rapid change justifies a different term.
The passage mentions metaphor or transference as the basis.
