⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث الفصل الأول : في الكيفيات المحسوسة [ الفصل الأول في الكيفيات المحسوسة ] • قدمها لأنها أظهر الأقسام الأربعة ( وهي إن كانت راسخة) أي ثابتة في موضوعها، بحيث يعسر زوالها عنه كصفرة الذهب وحلاوة العسل ( سميت انفعاليات وإلا ) وإن لم تكن راسخة كصفرة الوجل وجمرة الخجل ( فانفعالات وإنما سميت ) الكيفيات ( الأولى بذلك ) الاسم الذي هو الانفعاليات ( لوجهين الأول : أنها محسوسة والإحساس انفعال للحاسة فهي سبب للانفعال ومتبوعة .له ( الثاني : أنها تابعة للمزاج) التابع للانفعال. (إما بشخصها كحلاوة العسل فإنها تكونت فيه بسبب مزاجه الذي حدث بانفعال وقع في مادته ( أو بنوعها كحرارة النار، فإنها إن كانت ثابتة لبسيط ) لا يتصور فيه انفعال. (فقد توجد ) الحرارة التي هي نوعها ( في بعض المركبات تابعة للمزاج كالعسل والفلفل فإن حرارتهما تابعة لمزاجهما المستفاد من انفعال وقع في موادهما، ولما كان القسم الأول متبوعاً للانفعال من وجه وتابعاً له من وجه آخر نسب إليه . ( ثم إنهم إنما سموا القسم الثاني انفعالات مع ثبوت هذين الوجهين فيها ) لأنها لسرعة زوالها أشبهت (الانفعالات والتأثرات المتجددة الغير القارة (فسميت بها بطريق المجاز أو النقل كذا في الشفاء. قوله : (ثم أشار كلام على سبيل الاستئناف أو عطف على قوله : لأنها لسرعة زوالها كأنه قيل : إذ هو لسرعة الخ وهو يشارك الخ . قوله : ( فحرم القسم الثاني على صيغة المجهول من حرمه الشيء يحرمه إذا منعه إياه كذا في الصحاح. وكان الظاهر فحرموه إلا أنه ترك الفاعل لعدم تعلق الغرض به . قوله : ( فنقص الخ) فعلى هذا لا استعارة ولا نقل. قوله : ( أو بنوعها كحرارة (النار مبني على المختار عند البعض من اتحاد الحرارات بالنوع أو المراد بالنوع أعم من النوع الإضافي .
The Third Observatory, First Chapter: On sensible qualities. [First Chapter on sensible qualities] • He presented them because they are the most apparent of the four categories. (When they are stable) i.e., firmly established in their subject such that their removal from it is difficult, like the yellowness of gold and the sweetness of honey, (they are called 'infi'aliyyat'; otherwise) if they are not stable, like the yellowness of fear and the redness of shame, (then they are 'infi'alat'. And they are called) the first qualities (by that) name which is 'infi'aliyyat' (for two reasons: first, that they are sensible, and sensation is an affection of the sense, so they are a cause of affection and are followed by it; second, that they are dependent on temperament) which is dependent on affection. (Either in their particular instance, like the sweetness of honey, for it came to be in it due to its temperament that arose from an affection that occurred in its matter; or in their type, like the heat of fire, for although it is stable in a simple substance) in which no affection can be conceived, (yet it is found) the heat which is its type (in some composites dependent on temperament, like honey and pepper, for their heat is dependent on their temperament derived from an affection that occurred in their materials. And since the first division is followed by affection in one respect and following it in another respect, it is attributed to it. (Then they named the second division 'infi'alat' despite the presence of these two aspects in them) because due to their rapid vanishing they resemble (the ever-renewed and non-stable affections and passivities, so they were named by them by way of metaphor or transference, as in al-Shifa. His statement: (Then he indicated speech by way of resumption or conjunction with his statement: 'because of their rapid vanishing' as if it were said: 'since it is for the rapidity etc.' and it shares etc. His statement: (So the second division was deprived) in the passive form from 'he deprived him of something' if he prevented him from it, as in al-Sihah. The apparent would have been 'they deprived it', but the agent was omitted because the purpose is not attached to it. His statement: (so something was diminished, etc.) therefore there is no metaphor or transference. His statement: (or in their type like the heat of fire) is based on the preferred view of some that heats are the same in type, or the intended meaning of 'type' is broader than relative type.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage defines sensible qualities and establishes their primary division. It begins by stating that sensible qualities are presented first because they are the most apparent of the four categories of quality. The main division is between stable qualities (rasikha) and unstable qualities. Stable qualities are firmly rooted in their subject (e.g., gold's yellowness) and are called infi'aliyyat; unstable qualities (e.g., a person's pallor from fear) are called infi'alat. The passage then gives two reasons for calling the stable ones infi'aliyyat: first, they are sensible, and sensation is an affection (infi'al) of the sense organ, so these qualities cause affection; second, they are dependent on temperament, which itself results from an affection in matter. The explanation elaborates that dependence can be specific (e.g., honey's sweetness arises from its particular temperament) or generic (e.g., heat as a type exists in simple substances like fire but also in composites as dependent on temperament). The second division (unstable) is called infi'alat despite also being sensible and temperament-dependent, because their rapid change makes them resemble ever-renewed affections; thus they are named metaphorically. The passage concludes with textual notes clarifying linguistic points and the basis for the distinction regarding heat types.

كيفية محسوسة
راسخة
انفعاليات
انفعالات
مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that 'infi'aliyyat' means the quality itself is an affection or passive state.
Correction: The passage clarifies that stable sensible qualities are called infi'aliyyat because they cause affection in the senses and depend on temperament that results from an affection, not because they are themselves affections.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two reasons for calling stable sensible qualities 'infi'aliyyat'?
A) They are stable and easy to remove.
B) They are sensible and dependent on temperament.
C) They are abstract and universal.
D) They are neither sensible nor dependent on temperament.

Key Takeaway
Sensible qualities are divided into stable (infi'aliyyat) and unstable (infi'alat) based on their permanence and their relation to affection and temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your fleeting emotions like shame or fear – are they like the 'infi'alat' that vanish quickly? How much of your identity is built on such unstable qualities rather than stable virtues like patience or honesty?
?
The passage says stable qualities depend on 'temperament' (mizaj). What is the 'temperament' of your heart – is it disposed to lasting good or passing whims? What would it take to change your inner temperament?
?
If your stable qualities cause sensation in others, what do people sense from you? Do they perceive the 'sweetness of honey' or the 'heat of pepper'? Are you leaving a lasting impression of faith or a fleeting one?

Primary Effect of Heat: Ascension and Secondary Effects
States heat's primary effect is ascension with necessary consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that heat's primary effect is ascension (upward movement), which necessarily leads to gathering and separation, and also produces rarefaction and condensation as secondary effects.

Core Idea
Heat's first act is always upward, not expansion.

In this passage, the commentator summarizes the key findings about heat from the earlier discussion. He establishes that the primary effect of heat is to cause upward motion (tas'eed), with the separation of dissimilar parts and the gathering of similar parts following necessarily. He also cites Ibn Sina's definition and notes additional effects: rarefaction from the category of quality and condensation from the category of position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and find the statement that declares the primary effect of heat. Note that it is explicitly called 'the first act' (al-fi'l al-awwal).
The primary effect is ascension (tas'eed), i.e., upward movement.
2
Understand the causal process
Follow the explanation of how heat in a composite body causes separation of parts by moving the more ready for ascension first, then slower parts, followed by the rebellious, leading to separation and then gathering by nature.
This shows that gathering and separation are necessary consequences of the primary effect.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's definition
Look at the citation from Ibn Sina's Book of Definitions: heat is an active quality (kaifiyya fi'liyya) that makes its locus an agent of heating neighboring bodies, causing upward movement and lightness.
This definition supports the primary effect of ascension.
4
Recognize secondary effects
Identify the additional effects of heat: rarefaction (tahlukhul) from the category of quality (thinness of consistency) and condensation (takathuf) from the category of position (interlocking of parts).
Note that condensation can also be considered from the category of quality when fine parts exit.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(تنبيه) على ما علم مما قررناه في تفسير الحرارة. وهو أنْ يقال : (الفعل الأول لها أي للحرارة هو (التصعيد) والتحريك إلى الفوق بسبب ما تفيده من الميل المصعد ) والجمع والتفريق لازمان (له فإنه إذا حدثت الحرارة في الجسم المركب بمجاورة النار مثلا تحرك الأقبل للتصعيد قبل الأبطأ، وتحرك الأبطأ قبل العاصي فيلزم من هذا تفرق تلك الأجزاء المتخالفة ثم أجناسها بمقتضى طباعها كما مر . ( ولذلك ) أي ولما ذكرنا من اجتماعها مع ( أن (الفعل الأول للحرارة هو التصعيد المستتبع للتفريق والجمع. قال ابن سينا :) في كتاب الحدود : إنها كيفية فعلية أي تجعل محلها فاعلاً لمثلها فيما يجاوره. فإنّ النار تسخن ما يجاورها (محركة لما تكون ( تلك الكيفية ) فيه إلى فوق لإحداثها الخفة ) المقتضية للصعود ( فيحدث عنه ( أي عن التحريك إلى فوق وهو التصعيد (إن تفرق ) الحرارة ( المختلفات وتجمع المتماثلات ( لما عرفت . ( وتحدث ) أي ومن أحوال الحرارة أنها تحدث ) تخلخلاً من باب الكيف، وهو رقة القوام، ويقابله باب الكيف وهو غلظ القوام . (و) تحدث أيضاً ( تكاثفاً من باب الوضع ) وهو اندماج الأجزاء المتحدة بالطبع واجتماعها بحيث يخرج الجسم الغريب عما التكاثف من باب الوضع قيل : ويحدث التكاثف من باب الكيف في هذه الصورة أيضاً لأن الأجزاء اللطيفة إذا خرجت من البين فلا شك في حصول غلظ القوام للباقي فتأمل .
(A note) on what has been known from our exposition in the interpretation of heat. And it is to say: (The first act of it, i.e., of heat, is ascension (tas'eed) and movement upward due to the upward inclination it produces.) And gathering and separating are necessary consequences (of it. For when heat occurs in a composite body by adjacency to fire, for example, the more ready for ascension moves before the slower, and the slower moves before the rebellious, so from this follows the separation of those different parts, then their kinds, according to their natures, as has passed. (And therefore), i.e., because of what we mentioned of its conjunction with the fact that (the first act of heat is ascension which entails separation and gathering, Ibn Sina said:) in the Book of Definitions: It is a quality that is active (kaifiyya fi'liyya), i.e., it makes its locus an agent of its like in what adjoins it. So the fire heats what adjoins it, (moving that in which that quality is upward to produce lightness) which necessitates ascension (so from that occurs) (i.e., from the upward movement, which is ascension) (the separation) by heat (of different things and the gathering of similar things) as you know. (And it produces), i.e., among the states of heat is that it produces (rarefaction from the category of quality (kayf), which is thinness of consistency, and its opposite is from the category of quality, which is thickness of consistency. (And) it also produces (condensation from the category of position (waḍ'), which is the interlocking of parts united by nature and their gathering such that the foreign body exits from it. Condensation from the category of position – it is said: And it also produces condensation from the category of quality in this case as well, because when the fine parts exit from the midst, there is no doubt about the occurrence of thickness of consistency for the remainder, so consider.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a summary (tanbīh) of the previous analysis of heat. It declares that the first (primary) effect (al-fi'l al-awwal) of heat is ascension (tas'eed), i.e., upward movement, due to the upward inclination (al-mayl al-mus'id) that heat imparts. The secondary effects of gathering (jamm') and separation (tafrīq) are necessary consequences of this primary effect. The commentator explains the process: when heat affects a composite body, the parts that are more receptive to ascension move first, then slower parts, then those that resist, leading to separation of different types and then, by their natures, to gathering of similar types. He then cites Ibn Sina's definition from Kitab al-Hudud: heat is an active quality (kaifiyya fi'liyya) that makes its substrate an agent of similar effects on neighboring bodies. Thus fire heats its surroundings and moves the body in which that quality exists upward, producing lightness (khiffa) that necessitates ascension. From this upward movement (tas'eed), heat causes separation of dissimilar things and gathering of similar things. Additionally, heat produces two other effects: tahlukhul (rarefaction), which is a qualitative thinning of consistency (riqqa al-qawām), and its opposite is a qualitative thickening (ghilazz al-qawām). Heat also produces takathuf (condensation) from the category of position (waḍ'), which is the interlocking and gathering of naturally united parts, expelling any foreign body. There is also a note that condensation can also be from the category of quality when fine parts leave, leaving the remainder thicker.

الجمع
التفريق
التخلخل
التكاثف
الكيفية الفعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That heat's primary effect is expansion or rarefaction.
Correction: According to this passage, heat's primary effect is ascension (upward motion), with rarefaction and condensation as secondary effects, not primary.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the primary (first) effect of heat?
A) Rarefaction (tahlukhul)
B) Condensation (takathuf)
C) Ascension (tas'eed)
D) Separation of particles

Key Takeaway
Heat's primary effect is upward movement (tas'eed), which necessarily leads to separation of unlike parts and gathering of like parts; it also produces rarefaction and condensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul has 'first movements'—like heat's first act is ascension. What is that primary impulse in your heart when you wake each day? Is it toward Allah or away from Him? How can you train that first movement to be one of submission?
?
The passage shows that heat separates unlike parts and gathers like parts. In your life, what 'heat' (trials, ambitions, relationships) has exposed what is truly in you—separating what does not belong or drawing you closer to what is similar in faith? Are you content with what is being gathered?
?
If Allah has embedded natural orders like heat's effects into the universe, can you trust that He also has an order for your life—even when parts seem chaotic? Are you willing to surrender to that order, or do you resist the 'upward pull' toward obedience?

Interpreting 'Active' in Ibn Sina's Definition of Heat
Defense of 'active' and critique of its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends Ibn Sina's use of 'active' (fi'liyya) against al-Imam's objection of redundancy, and why the contextual interpretation of 'active' is disputed.

Core Idea
Active means influencing without being influenced, not a redundant qualifier.

The first gloss tackles a criticism from al-Imam that the term 'active' in Ibn Sina's definition of heat is redundant. The commentator shows how a specific interpretation refutes this, but questions the basis of that interpretation. This lesson unpacks the debate over what 'active quality' truly means.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on 'it makes its locus'
Begin with the opening phrase: 'His statement: (i.e., it makes its locus, etc.)'. Notice the commentator's claim that this interpretation repels al-Imam's objection.
Al-Imam's objection is that 'active' is superfluous.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'active'
The commentator distinguishes between 'active' as interpreted by the shārīḥ (which lacks contextual evidence) and the correct meaning: that which necessitates influence only, not being influenced.
Compare with 'passive' (infi'āliyya).
3
Reflect on the debate
Consider why context is important. The commentator argues that without contextual indication, the shārīḥ's interpretation is arbitrary. Ask yourself: which reading is more faithful to Ibn Sina?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أي تجعل محلها (الخ اندفع بهذا التفسير ما قاله الإمام من أن قوله فعلية مستدرك ) لكن تفسير الفعلية بما ذكره الشارح قدس سره مما لا قرينة عليه فإنَّ الفعلية في مقابلة الانفعالية هي التي تقتضي التأثير فقط ولا توجب تأثراً . قوله : ( الفعل الأول لها التصعيد سياق كلامه يدل على أنَّ الفعل الأول لها التخفيف أي إحداث الخفة فأولية التصعيد بالقياس إلى الجمع والتفريق . قوله : (قال ابن سينا في كتاب الحدود إنها كيفية فعلية محركة قال الإمام في المباحث المشرقية : واعلم أنَّ قوله : كيفية فعلية محركة فيه نظر. لأن المراد من الكيفية الفعلية الكيفية التي تؤثر في أمر ما والمفهوم من المحرك أنه الذي يؤثر في أمر ما هو الحركة فيكون الدال على مفيد الحركة دالاً بالتضمن على المفيد المطلق . فقوله : كيفية فعلية محركة نازلة منزلة ما يقال : إنه جوهر جسماني حيواني في كونه مكرراً فالأولى حذفه .
His statement: (i.e., it makes its locus, etc.) – by this interpretation, what al-Imam said is repelled: that his statement 'active' is redundant. But the interpretation of 'active' (fi'liyya) as the commentator (may his soul be sanctified) mentioned lacks any contextual indication; for 'active' in contrast to 'passive' (infi'āliyya) is that which necessitates influence only and does not require being influenced. His statement: (The first act of it is ascension) – the context of his words indicates that the first act of it is lightening (takhfīf), i.e., producing lightness, so the primacy of ascension is in relation to gathering and separating. His statement: (Ibn Sina said in the Book of Definitions: it is an active moving quality) – al-Imam said in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya: Know that his statement 'an active moving quality' requires examination. Because what is meant by 'active quality' is a quality that influences something, and the concept of 'moving' (muḥarrik) is that which influences something that is motion. Thus, that which indicates the production of motion indicates, by implication, the absolute producer. So his statement 'active moving quality' is like saying 'a bodily animal substance' in being repetitive, so it is better to delete it.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage collects three separate glosses (ta'līqāt) on the base text. The first gloss addresses the phrase 'i.e., it makes its locus' in Ibn Sina's definition. The commentator notes that this interpretation refutes al-Imam's objection that the term 'active' (fi'liyya) is redundant. However, the commentator points out that the interpretation of 'active' as given by the commentator (shārīḥ) lacks contextual evidence; rather, 'active' in contrast to 'passive' (infi'āliyya) means that which only necessitates effect (ta'thīr) without requiring to be affected itself. The second gloss discusses the phrase 'the first act of it is ascension.' The commentator clarifies that the context shows the first act is actually lightening (takhfīf), i.e., producing lightness, so ascension is primary only in relation to gathering and separating, not absolutely. The third gloss critiques Ibn Sina's full definition 'an active moving quality' (kaifiyya fi'liyya muḥarrika). Al-Imam from al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya argues that this is repetitive: 'active quality' already implies influencing something, and 'moving' specifically implies influencing motion, so the combination is redundant, like saying 'a bodily animal substance.' Thus, he recommends deleting the word 'muḥarrika' (moving).

Key Terms
الفعلية
الانفعالية
المستدرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct meaning of 'active' (fi'liyya) as opposed to 'passive' (infi'āliyya)?
A) That which influences and is influenced
B) That which only necessitates influence and does not require being influenced
C) That which makes its locus hot
D) That which is redundant

Key Takeaway
The term 'active' in Ibn Sina's definition is not redundant if understood in its technical sense: a quality that influences without being influenced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator insists that 'active' means influencing without being influenced. In your own life, are there areas where you allow others to sway your commitment to truth? What would it cost you to be truly 'active' in your faith, influencing others without being corrupted by their doubts?
?
Al-Imam dismissed 'active' as redundant—a word added without purpose. Have you ever dismissed a religious concept as unnecessary because you lacked the proper context? Which of your current beliefs might need re-examination to ensure you haven't committed the same mistake?
?
The correction hinges on precise language. When you speak about your faith, do you use words carelessly, or do you strive for precision as the scholars do? Commit to one specific change in how you discuss Islamic concepts this week.

The First Act of Heat: Lightening, Not Ascension
Clarifying the primary effect of heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator interprets the first act of heat as lightening (takhfīf) rather than ascension, and how ascension is only primary in relation to gathering and separating.

Core Idea
Heat first lightens its subject; ascension follows secondarily.

The second gloss clarifies the phrase 'the first act of it is ascension' from Ibn Sina's definition. The commentator argues that the context suggests the primary effect is lightening (making lighter), and ascension is only first relative to the subsequent actions of gathering and separating.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on 'the first act of it is ascension'
The commentator says the context indicates the first act is lightening (takhfīf), i.e., producing lightness. Ascension is only first in relation to gathering and separating.
Ascension (su'ūd) is a motion upward, while lightening is the change that causes it.
2
Understand the causal sequence
Heat causes lightness (takhfīf), which then causes ascension. But ascension is the visible motion; however, causally, lightness precedes.
3
Reflect on the primacy
The commentator is precise: he says 'first act' refers to lightening. The primacy of ascension is only from the perspective of gathering and separating (i.e., the next steps in the process).
This shows careful attention to the order of effects.

Redundancy in Ibn Sina's 'Active Moving Quality'
Critique of redundancy in Ibn Sina's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Imam's argument that Ibn Sina's phrase 'active moving quality' is redundant because 'active' already entails influencing motion, and you will evaluate whether the deletion of 'moving' is justified.

Core Idea
'Active' already implies moving; adding 'moving' is like saying 'bodily animal substance'.

The third gloss presents al-Imam's sharp critique: Ibn Sina's definition of heat as 'an active moving quality' is repetitive. Al-Imam argues that 'active quality' already covers influencing, and 'moving' is just a specific instance, so the combination is like saying 'a bodily animal substance'—a clear redundancy. This lesson explores the logical basis of this criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Imam's argument
Al-Imam states that 'active quality' means influencing something, and 'moving' means influencing motion. Since motion is a type of 'something', 'moving' is already included in 'active', making the phrase repetitive.
He gives an analogy: 'bodily animal substance' is repetitive because 'animal' already implies body.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Consider the relationship: 'active' is a genus, 'moving' is a species. A definition that includes both is like defining man as 'animal rational animal'—redundant.
3
Evaluate the proposed deletion
Al-Imam suggests deleting 'mover' (muḥarrik). Would that leave a sufficient definition? Is there any loss of specificity? Reflect on whether 'active quality' alone captures the essence of heat.
This touches on the criteria for a proper definition.

Ibn Sina's Definition of Heat and al-Razi's Critique
Ibn Sina's definition of heat and al-Razi's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of heat as an active quality and al-Razi's objection that it is redundantly phrased.

Core Idea
Al-Razi shows that Ibn Sina's definition of heat is tautological.

This lesson examines a seminal definition of heat from Ibn Sina's Book of Definitions, followed by a sharp logical critique from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. Al-Razi argues that the qualifier 'moving' is already implied by 'active quality,' making the phrase redundant. Understanding this critique sharpens one's appreciation for precision in theological and philosophical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the two distinct voices: Ibn Sina and al-Razi.
The passage has two parts: definition and objection.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's definition
Focus on what Ibn Sina means by 'active quality' and 'moving' and the example of fire.
Heat makes its subject an agent of its like in adjacent things.
3
Analyze al-Razi's objection
Identify the redundancy argument. Why does al-Razi say it's like 'corporeal animal substance'?
An active quality already implies causing an effect, so 'moving' is redundant.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider the importance of precise wording in definitions. What does this teach about logical consistency?
Even great philosophers can be imprecise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا في كتاب الحدود: إنها كيفية فعلية أي تجعل محلها فاعلاً لمثلها فيما يجاوره. فإنّ النار تسخن ما يجاورها (محركة لما تكون ( تلك الكيفية ) فيه إلى فوق لإحداثها الخفة ) المقتضية للصعود ( فيحدث عنه ( أي عن التحريك إلى فوق وهو التصعيد (إن تفرق ) الحرارة ( المختلفات وتجمع المتماثلات). قال الإمام في المباحث المشرقية: واعلم أنَّ قوله: كيفية فعلية محركة فيه نظر. لأن المراد من الكيفية الفعلية الكيفية التي تؤثر في أمر ما والمفهوم من المحرك أنه الذي يؤثر في أمر ما هو الحركة فيكون الدال على مفيد الحركة دالاً بالتضمن على المفيد المطلق. فقوله: كيفية فعلية محركة نازلة منزلة ما يقال: إنه جوهر جسماني حيواني في كونه مكرراً فالأولى حذفه.
Ibn Sina said in the Book of Definitions: It (heat) is an active quality, i.e., it makes its subject an agent of its like in what is adjacent to it. For fire heats what is adjacent to it, moving that in which this quality is upward, because it produces lightness necessitating ascent. Hence there occurs (from that movement upward, which is rarefaction) the separation of different things and the aggregation of similar things. The Imam (al-Razi) said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: Know that his statement "an active quality moving" is questionable. Because what is meant by "active quality" is a quality that affects something, and the meaning of "moving" is that which affects something that is movement. So the expression indicating that it produces movement indicates by entailment that it is a cause in an absolute sense. So his statement "an active quality moving" is like saying "a corporeal animal substance" in being redundant. So it is better to delete it.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's definition of heat and a critical objection. First, Ibn Sina defines heat as an active quality (كيفية فعلية), meaning it makes its subject (the hot body) an agent that produces a similar quality in adjacent bodies. For example, fire heats what is near it. He further describes heat as moving (محركة) that which it is in upward because it produces lightness, which necessitates ascent. As a result, heat causes separation of different things and aggregation of similar things. Then al-Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (the author of al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya) criticizes this definition. He argues that the phrase "active quality moving" is redundant. An "active quality" is already understood as a quality that affects something; "moving" is a specific kind of affecting, so it is like saying "a corporeal animal substance" – the second term is implied by the first. Hence, Ibn Sina's wording is tautological and should be omitted. The objection highlights a logical nuance in defining heat.

Key Terms
كيفية فعلية
محركة
التصعيد
الإمام
المباحث المشرقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's definition of heat is fully precise and unambiguous.
Correction: Al-Razi demonstrates that the qualifier 'moving' is logically redundant, rendering the phrasing less precise.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's criticism of Ibn Sina's definition of heat?
A) It is too vague
B) It is redundant because 'active quality' already implies 'moving'
C) It contradicts Aristotelian physics
D) It does not mention rarefaction

Key Takeaway
Precision in definition is essential; redundant terms weaken logical clarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a definition that seemed precise but was actually redundant? How does this lesson challenge you to be more careful in your own reasoning about fundamental concepts?
?
If precision in defining terms is so important, what does it say about your own understanding of the names and attributes of Allah? Are you willing to scrutinize your own language to avoid tautology?
?
Al-Razi did not hesitate to critique a great scholar like Ibn Sina. Is there an area of your faith where you have accepted a formulation without questioning its logical soundness? Will you now examine it?

The Definition and Function of Innate Heat
Defines innate heat and presents al-Razi's view on its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core definition and protective role of innate heat and the nuanced view of al-Razi regarding its relationship with fire heat.

Core Idea
Innate heat is a balanced, integrated heat that preserves life, not just ordinary fire.

This lesson defines innate heat, explaining its function of protecting the body's innate moistures from foreign heat. It also presents the major debate over whether innate heat is essentially the same as fire heat or different, with Imam al-Razi offering a detailed perspective that clarifies the distinction based on balance and inclusion within the living compound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage uses technical terms like 'innate heat' and 'fire heat'.
2
Identify the function of innate heat
Notice how innate heat is said to protect innate moistures from foreign heat. This protective role is central to its definition.
3
Understand al-Razi's argument
Al-Razi argues that fire heat can become innate if it is balanced and integrated into the compound. The difference is not in essence but in being inside or outside.
This reconciles the view that they are of one genus with the observation that they behave differently.

فالحرارة الغريزية تحمي الرطوبات الغريزية عن أن تستولي عليها الحرارة الغريزية كالحرارة النارية، فهي مخالفة لها في الماهية ومنهم من جعلهما أي الغريزية والنارية من جنس أي نوع واحد. فإن الإمام الرازي قال: والذي عندي أن النار إذا خالطت سائر العناصر وأفادتها طبخاً ونضجاً واعتدالاً وقواماً ولم تبلغ في الكثرة إلى حيث تبطل قوامها وتحرقها، ولم تكن في القلة بحيث تعجز عن الطبخ الموجب للاعتدال فحرارتها هي الحرارة الغريزية. وإنما كانت دافعة للحر الغريب لأنَّ ذلك الغريب يحاول التفريق وتلك الحرارة الغريزية أفادت المركب من الطبخ والنضج ما يعسر معه على الحرارة الغريبة تفريق أجزائه فالتفاوت بين الغريزية والغريبة النارية ليس في الماهية بل في كون الغريزية داخلة في ذلك المركب دون تلك الغريبة حتى لو توهمنا الغريبة داخلة فيه. أجزائها. قوله: الرطوبات الغريزية وهي الحاصلة في بدن الحي بعد تفاعل العناصر. قوله: ومنهم من جعلهما إلخ إليه ذهب جالينوس وتبعه الأطباء. قوله: بل في كون الغريزية إلخ أي قائمة بما هو داخل في المركب موجب لالتئام. قوله: فإنها آلة للطبيعة الطبيعة قد تطلق على النفس باعتبار تدبيرها للبدن على التسخير لا الاختيار وهو المراد هاهنا وقد تطلق على الصورة النوعية للبسائط كما سيأتي في مباحث القدرة. قوله: ومنهم من جعلهما أي الغريزية والنارية من جنس ورد بأن الحرارة الغريزية تفارق بالموت دون الأسطقسية كما يدرك في بشرته، ولذا يتعفن بدنه وينتفخ انتفاخاً عظيماً ولو كان وسط الجمد والثلج فهما متغايران قطعاً. وحكي عن أرسطو أنَّ الحرارة الغريزية من جنس الحرارة التي تفيض من الأجرام السماوية فإنه إذا امتزجت العناصر وانكسرت سورة كيفياتها حصل للمركب نوع وحدة وبساطة بها يناسب البسائط السماوية ففاضت عليه مزاج معتدل به حفظ التركيب وحرارة غريزية بها قوام الحياة وقبول علاقة النفس.
Then innate heat protects the innate moistures from being overcome by foreign heat like fire heat, so it is different in essence from it, and some of them consider the two—i.e., innate and fire—to be of one genus, i.e., one type. Indeed, Imam al-Razi said: 'My opinion is that fire, when it mixes with the other elements and gives them cooking, ripening, moderation, and consistency, and does not reach such abundance as to destroy their consistency and burn them, nor such scarcity as to be incapable of the cooking that brings about moderation, then its heat is innate heat. And it repels foreign heat only because that foreign heat seeks to separate, and that innate heat has given the compound from cooking and ripening what makes it difficult for the foreign heat to separate its parts. So the difference between innate and foreign fire heat is not in essence but in the fact that innate heat is included in that compound while foreign is not, even if we suppose the foreign were included in it.' Its parts. He said: 'Innate moistures are those that occur in the body of the living being after the interaction of the elements.' He said: 'And some of them consider the two, etc.': Galen held this view, followed by the physicians. He said: 'Rather, in the fact that innate is included, etc.': meaning it is established as what is inside the compound, causing cohesion. He said: 'For it is an instrument of nature': 'nature' may be applied to the soul in terms of its managing the body by compulsion, not choice, and that is intended here, and it may be applied to the specific form of simple bodies, as will come in the investigations of power. He said: 'And some of them consider the two, i.e., innate and fire, of one genus': it is refuted by the fact that innate heat departs at death while elemental heat does not, as is perceived in one's skin, and therefore his body decays and swells greatly even if it is in the middle of ice and snow, so they are certainly different. And it is reported from Aristotle that innate heat is of the kind of heat that emanates from celestial bodies, because when the elements mix and the intensity of their qualities is broken, the compound obtains a kind of unity and simplicity by which it resembles celestial simples, so a moderate temperament flows upon it, by which the composition is preserved, and an innate heat by which life subsists and the attachment of the soul is accepted.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This is a definition and classification passage. It begins by stating that innate heat protects innate moistures from being overtaken by foreign heat (like fire heat), and then presents different views on whether innate heat and fire heat are of the same genus or different. The passage first mentions that some consider them of one type, then cites al-Razi's detailed opinion that fire heat can be considered innate when it is properly balanced and integrated into the compound, and the difference is not in essence but in being inside or outside the compound. Then a series of glosses (qāla) explain key terms: 'innate moistures' are defined, Galen's view is noted, the phrase 'rather in being included' is explained as meaning it is part of the compound's cohesion, and 'nature' is defined. A refutation follows: innate heat departs at death while elemental heat remains, proving they are different. Finally, Aristotle's view is reported: innate heat is from the celestial spheres, and when elements mix, a moderate temperament arises that preserves the compound and gives life. This establishes the doctrine that innate heat is a special, compound-specific heat.

Key Terms
الحرارة الغريزية (al-ḥarāra al-gharīziyya)
الحرارة النارية (al-ḥarāra al-nāriyya)
الرطوبات الغريزية (al-ruṭūbāt al-gharīziyya)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Innate heat is exactly the same as ordinary fire heat.
Correction: Innate heat is a balanced, integrated heat that sustains life; it differs in behavior and essence from raw fire heat, though it can arise from fire heat when balanced.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what determines whether fire heat is considered innate heat?
A) Its temperature
B) Its inclusion and balance within a compound
C) Its source
D) Its ability to burn

Key Takeaway
Innate heat is defined by its protective function and balanced integration within the living compound, not merely by its source; it can arise from fire heat when properly balanced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'foreign heat'—distractions, unhealthy habits, or overwhelming worries—is currently trying to penetrate and disrupt the balance in your life? Can you identify one specific 'innate heat'—a stable practice or relationship—that protects your inner resilience? Will you consciously strengthen that one today?
?
Al-Razi teaches that balance turns ordinary fire into life-giving warmth. Is there a raw, untamed passion or energy in you that needs to be 'cooked and ripened' into moderation before it can become a source of strength rather than destruction? What step will you take to bring that part of yourself into proper balance with the rest of your compound?

Evidence for the Distinct Nature of Innate Heat
Presents a refutation and Aristotle's alternative view on the origin of innate heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key evidence that proves innate heat is distinct from elemental fire heat, including its departure at death and its celestial origin as per Aristotle.

Core Idea
Innate heat leaves at death, proving it is not ordinary fire; Aristotle ties it to celestial heat.

This lesson presents the counterarguments and evidence that innate heat is distinct from fire heat. It begins with a refutation based on the observation that innate heat departs at death while elemental heat remains. Then it reports Aristotle's theory that innate heat originates from celestial bodies, which gives life and allows the soul to attach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
This passage contains several glosses. First, understand each gloss (definition of moistures, mention of Galen, explanation of inclusion, and definition of nature). Then focus on the refutation and Aristotle's view.
The refutation is the main argument: innate heat departs at death, proving it is different.
2
Understand the refutation based on death
The passage argues that because innate heat leaves the body at death (causing decay even in cold), it must be different from elemental fire heat which remains in the body.
3
Grasp Aristotle's celestial origin theory
Aristotle proposes that innate heat is a type of heat that emanates from celestial bodies, which flows into the compound when elements achieve harmony, giving life and allowing the soul to attach.

?

?

Definition of Coldness and the Sensibility Objection
Definition of coldness with condition and refutation of sensibility objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why coldness is defined as the privation of heat in a subject capable of being hot, and how the objection that coldness is sensible is refuted.

Core Idea
Coldness is not mere absence; it is a real quality.

In this passage, the author presents a definition of coldness as the absence of heat from something that is naturally capable of being hot, excluding celestial bodies. He then addresses an objection: if coldness is merely an absence, how can it be sensed? The refutation uses the example of water to show that coldness behaves like a real quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and condition
Note that coldness is not just any absence of heat, but specifically from something apt to be hot, to exclude the sphere.
2
Understand the opposition type
The relation between heat and cold is privation and possession (al-ʿadam wa-al-malaka).
3
Examine the objection
The objector says coldness cannot be an absence because it is sensible.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author argues that what is sensed is not coldness itself but the substance; however, the variation of coldness shows it is a real quality.
5
Consider the water example
Water's coldness intensifies and disappears while water remains, proving coldness is not a non-entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسها : البرودة قيل : ( هي ( عدم الحرارة ) لا مطلقا بل (عما من شأنه أن يكون حاراً واعتبر هذا القيد (احترازاً عن الفلك ) فإنَّ عدم حرارته لا تسمى برودة إذ ليس شأنه أن يكون حاراً. وعلى هذا ) فالتقابل بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة ويبطله) من أي هذا القول ( أنها ) أعني البرودة (محسوسة ) كالحرارة (والعدم لا يحس) بالضرورة ) لا يقال : المحسوس) حال عدم الحرارة ليس هو البرودة بل هو ( ذات الجسم لأن البرد يشتد ويضعف ويعدم وذات الجسم باقية بحالها. فإنا نحس من الماء برداً شديداً جداً ثم يضعف ذلك البرد شيئاً فشيئاً إلى أن ينعدم بالكلية مع أن الماء باق في هذه الأحوال على جوهره الذاتي فلا تكون البرودة أمراً عدمياً (بل
Fifth: Coldness. It is said: It is absence of heat, not absolutely, but (absence of heat) from that which is apt to be hot. This condition is considered (as a guard against the sphere), for its absence of heat is not called coldness since it is not apt to be hot. According to this, the opposition between them is opposition of privation and possession. And this is invalidated by the fact that this statement (that) coldness is sensible like heat, and absence is not sensed necessarily. It cannot be said: The sensed thing in the state of absence of heat is not coldness but rather the substance of the body, because cold intensifies and weakens and disappears while the substance of the body remains. For we sense from water very intense cold, then that cold weakens little by little until it disappears completely while the water remains in these states on its essential substance; so coldness is not a non-existent thing (bl[?]
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage defines coldness (al-burūda) within the context of natural philosophy. It begins by stating the fifth [property?] is coldness. The definition given is that coldness is the absence of heat, but not an absolute absence; rather, it is the absence of heat from something that is by its nature capable of being hot. The qualification 'from that which is apt to be hot' is added to exclude the celestial spheres, whose lack of heat is not called coldness because they are not naturally hot. The author notes that under this definition, the relation between heat and cold is one of privation and possession (ʿadam wa-malaka), i.e., coldness is the privation of heat in a subject that can possess heat. Then an objection is raised: coldness is sensible (like heat), but a mere absence cannot be sensed. The refutation responds that what is sensed when heat is absent is not coldness per se but rather the substance of the body. However, the objector persists: coldness varies in intensity and can disappear while the substance remains, proving it is not a pure privation. The passage gives the example of water: we sense very intense cold, then it weakens and vanishes while the water's essence remains unchanged, indicating coldness is not a mere non-existence (ʿadamī). The argument demonstrates that coldness is a real quality, not just absence.

Key Terms
البرودة
الحرارة
العدم
الملكة
التقابل
ذات الجسم
الجوهر الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Coldness is merely the absence of heat, like an empty space.
Correction: Coldness is a real quality that can be sensed and varies, not a pure privation.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage reject the objection that coldness cannot be sensible because it is an absence?
A) Because absence can be sensed indirectly.
B) Because coldness is not an absence but a positive quality.
C) Because the sensed cold is actually the substance of the body, not coldness itself.
D) Because the example of water shows coldness is a non-existent thing.

Key Takeaway
Coldness is defined as the privation of heat in a capable subject, but it is not a mere absence because it is sensible and varies, as illustrated by the example of water.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience cold, do you perceive it as a positive sensation or merely as a lack of heat? How might this distinction affect your awareness of Allah's creation of qualities and their opposites?
?
The passage argues that even a privation can be sensed and real. In your spiritual life, what 'absence' do you sense most keenly – a lack of patience, gratitude, or closeness to Allah? How does sensing that privation point to the reality of the quality you are missing?

The True Nature of Moisture: Ease of Adhesion and Separation
Definition of moisture as ease of adhesion and separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of moisture as a quality of ease of adhesion and separation, and why it is not merely stickiness.

Core Idea
Moisture is not just stickiness; it is the quality of both easy sticking and easy separation.

In this passage, the author begins the second objective on moisture and dryness, establishing the correct definition of moisture. The definition is subtle: it is a quality that makes a thing both stick to others and separate from them easily. This distinction is crucial to avoid common misunderstandings and to address later objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the opening segment where moisture is defined as 'ease of adhesion and ease of separation'.
Notice that the definition includes both adhesion and separation.
2
Understand the commentary on quality vs. relation
The commentator explains that 'ease of adhesion' is a metaphorical expression, because ease is a relation, but moisture is a quality. The real meaning is a quality that necessitates that ease.
This prevents a trivial objection.
3
Examine the counterexample of dry powder
The commentary addresses an objection: if moisture is ease of adhesion, then finely ground dry powder would be moist. The answer involves mixing with air particles, but it depends on a view that the author does not hold.
This shows the precision required in definition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : في الرطوبة واليبوسة الحق أنها كيفية موجودة ( مضادة للحرارة) من شأنها أن تجمع بين وغيرها كما نقلناه عن ابن سينا . [ المقصد الثاني : في الرطوبة واليبوسة ]

في الرطوبة واليبوسة وفيهما مباحث . أحدها : الرطوبة سهولة الالتصاق ) أي كيفية تقتضي سهولة الالتصاق بالغير ( و ) سهولة ( الانفصال ) عنه هذا هو المختار في تفسير الرطوبة عند الإمام الرازي قال ابن سينا : ( إذا كانت الرطوبة عبارة عما ذكر ( فيجب أن يكون الأشد التصاقاً أرطب ( مما هو أضعف التصاقاً لأنه إذا كان الالتصاق معلولاً للرطوبة كان شدته وقوته دالة على شدة علته وقوتها ( وذلك يوجب أن يكون العسل أرطب من الماء لأنَّ العسل أشد التصاقاً منه فإنا إذا غمسنا فيه الأصبع كان ما يلزمه منه أكثر مما يلزمه من الماء وأشد التصاقا به منه وكذا الحان في الدهن ولا

قوله : (أي كيفية إلخ) يعني أن تفسير الرطوبة بما ذكر قول مجازي لان الالتصاق وسهولته من الإضافة والرطوبة ليست منها والمراد كيفية تقتضي ذلك فلا يرد ما قيل : إِنَّ الرطوبة لو كانت عبارة عن سهولة الالتصاق لوجب أن يكون اليابس المدقوق دقاً ناعماً رطباً لكونه كذلك لا في سهولة التصاقه بسبب تصغر أجزائه، والتصغر ليست بكيفية. وأما ما قيل من أن التصاقه بواسطة مخالطة الأجزاء الهوائية فليس بشيء، لأن من فسر الرطوبة بسهولة الالتصاق لا يقول برطوبة الهواء فلا هو يصح هذا الجواب من قبله

قوله : ( قال ابن سينا إلخ) في الشفاء ما حاصله أنَّ بعض الأجسام الرطبة إذا فتشنا أحواله نجد فيه التصاقا بما يماسه فالجمهور ظنوا أن الرطوبة هي الالتصاق وليس كذلك. وإلا لكان ما أشد التصاقاً أرطب. فيلزم أن يكون الدهن والعسل أرطب من الماء. قال الإمام : هذا إنما يلزم لو فسر الرطوبة بنفس الالتصاق لكنها عبارة عن سهولة الالتصاق بالغير مع سهولة الانفصال عنه ولا شك أن الماء أكمل في هذا المعنى وبما نقلناه ظهر لك أنَّ اعتراض ابن سينا على من جعل الرطوبة نفس الالتصاق وإن تغيير الإمام تفسير الجمهور إلى تفسيره لدفع الاعتراض المذكور فإيراد المصنف اعتراض ابن سينا على تفسير الإمام والجواب عنه بما ذكره سهو

قوله : (لأنه إذا كان إلخ) التقريب غير تام لأنه لم يجعل الالتصاق معلولاً للرطوبة بل سهولته.

قوله : (أي كيفية تقتضي إلخ) فسر سهولة الالتصاق بهذا لأنَّ السهولة أمر نسبي وليس من مقولة الكيف وقد يعترض على اعتبار سهولة الالتصاق بأنه يوجب أن يكون اليابس المدقوق جداً کالعظام المحرقة رطباً لكونها كذلك. ويجاب بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لمخالطة الأجزاء الهوائية. وهذا إنما يتم على رأي من يقول برطوبة الهواء وسهولة التصاقه لولا مانع فرط اللطافة لا على رأي الإمام .
Second objective: On moisture and dryness. The truth is that it is an existing quality (opposed to heat) whose property is to combine between [things] and other, as we have narrated from Ibn Sina. [Second objective: On moisture and dryness]

On moisture and dryness, and in them are investigations. First: Moisture is ease of adhesion, i.e., a quality that necessitates ease of adhesion with another and ease of separation from it. This is the chosen interpretation of moisture according to Imam al-Razi. Ibn Sina said: 'If moisture is the expression of what has been mentioned, then whatever is more adhesive must be more moist than what is less adhesive, because if adhesion is an effect of moisture, its intensity and strength indicate the intensity and strength of its cause. And that necessitates that honey is moister than water, because honey is more adhesive; for when we dip a finger into it, what clings from it is more than what clings from water and more adhesive to it. Similarly [for] oil and not...'

His statement: 'i.e., a quality [etc.]' means that interpreting moisture as mentioned is a metaphorical expression, because adhesion and its ease are from the category of relation (al-iḍāfa), and moisture is not from it; rather the intended meaning is a quality that necessitates that. So it is not objected that if moisture were ease of adhesion, then dry powder finely ground would be moist because it is like that, due to ease of its adhesion because of the smallness of its parts, and smallness is not a quality. As for what is said that its adhesion is due to mixing with airy parts, it is nothing, because who interprets moisture as ease of adhesion does not affirm moisture of air, so this answer is not valid from him.

His statement: 'Ibn Sina said [etc.]' in al-Shifā, the summary is that some moist bodies when we investigate their states we find in them adhesion to what touches them. The majority thought that moisture is adhesion, but it is not so. Otherwise, whatever is more adhesive would be moister. Thus it would be necessary that oil and honey are moister than water. The Imam said: This would only follow if moisture were interpreted as the same as adhesion, but it is an expression of ease of adhesion with another along with ease of separation from it, and there is no doubt that water is more perfect in this meaning. From what we have narrated, it becomes clear to you that Ibn Sina's objection is against those who make moisture the same as adhesion, and that the Imam changed the interpretation of the majority to his interpretation to repel the aforementioned objection. So the author's bringing Ibn Sina's objection against the Imam's interpretation and the answer to it as he mentioned is an oversight.

His statement: 'because if it is [etc.]' the approximation is not complete, because he did not make adhesion an effect of moisture, but rather its ease.

His statement: 'i.e., a quality that necessitates [etc.]' he interpreted ease of adhesion by this, because ease is a relative matter and is not from the category of quality (kayf). It may be objected against considering ease of adhesion that it would necessitate that very finely ground dry things like burned bones are moist because they are like that. It is answered that it is possible that this is due to mixing with airy parts. And this is only valid on the opinion of those who affirm moisture of air and ease of its adhesion but for the obstacle of excessive subtlety, not on the opinion of the Imam.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of moisture (al-rutūba) and discusses an objection by Ibn Sina. The main text begins the second objective on moisture and dryness, stating that moisture is an existing quality opposed to heat, capable of combining things. It then presents the first investigation: moisture is defined as ease of adhesion (suhūlat al-iltiṣāq) to another and ease of separation from it – this is the interpretation preferred by Imam al-Razi. Ibn Sina objects that if moisture were defined as ease of adhesion, then whatever is more adhesive would be moister, leading to the conclusion that honey and oil are moister than water. The commentator explains that this objection only applies if one defines moisture as adhesion itself, not as ease of adhesion. Imam al-Razi’s definition includes both ease of adhesion and ease of separation, and water has greater ease of separation than honey. The commentator then discusses a potential counterexample: dry, ground powder adheres easily but is not moist; the answer is that this adhesion is due to mixing with air particles, but this answer depends on a view that affirms moisture of air, which al-Razi does not hold. The commentator further notes that the author's inclusion of Ibn Sina's objection against al-Razi's definition is an oversight because Ibn Sina's objection was aimed at those who identify moisture with adhesion itself, not at al-Razi's refined definition. The passage thus clarifies that moisture is a specific quality of ease, not mere adhesion.

الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moisture is simply stickiness or adhesion.
Correction: Moisture is a quality that causes both easy adhesion and easy separation, not just adhesion.

Checkpoint
According to the definition preferred by Imam al-Razi, moisture is:
A) Adhesion itself.
B) Ease of adhesion only.
C) A quality that causes ease of adhesion and ease of separation.
D) A relation between objects.

Key Takeaway
Moisture is a quality that makes a thing both stick to others and separate from them easily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a habit or relationship you hold onto tightly. Does your attachment allow for easy separation when needed, or is it a clinging that harms your faith? In light of this lesson's definition, what would true 'moisture' look like in that attachment? Commit to one step toward either healthy adhesion or gentle release.
?
The quality of moisture enables both sticking and letting go. Is your heart 'moist' toward Allah – able to hold fast to His commands yet ready to release worldly attachments with ease? Where do you find yourself either too dry to stick to good deeds or too stuck on sin to let go? Choose one area and ask Allah for the quality of balanced attachment.
?
Consider a time you held onto a grudge or pride. Did that adhesion feel easy or difficult to separate from? According to this lesson, true moisture implies both ease of adhesion and separation. Does your inability to let go reveal a lack of the quality of moisture in your soul? What is one thing you can do today to practice ease of separation?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Ibn Sina's Objection and the Refined Definition
Ibn Sina's objection regarding honey and the resolution via al-Razi's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's objection that honey would be moister than water, and how al-Razi's refined definition avoids it.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's objection only hits a crude view of moisture as mere adhesion, not al-Razi's nuanced definition.

Ibn Sina raised a clever objection: if moisture is ease of adhesion, then honey, being stickier, would be moister than water. The commentator explains that this objection only applies if one misinterprets moisture as adhesion itself, not as a quality that enables both adhesion and separation. This lesson walks through the objection, al-Razi's response, and the commentator's critique of the author's framing.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's objection
Read the Arabic and translation of Ibn Sina's argument: if moisture is defined as ease of adhesion, then the more adhesive a thing is, the moister it should be. Therefore honey is moister than water.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand al-Razi's response
The commentator quotes al-Razi: the objection only follows if moisture is interpreted as adhesion itself. But al-Razi defines it as ease of adhesion plus ease of separation. Water has greater ease of separation than honey.
The key is the inclusion of ease of separation.
3
Examine the commentator's critique of the author
The commentator notes that the author wrongly presents Ibn Sina's objection as directed against al-Razi's definition, but Ibn Sina was targeting those who identify moisture with adhesion.
This shows careful historical analysis.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina think honey should be moister than water, according to the objection?
A) Because honey is sweeter.
B) Because honey is more adhesive than water.
C) Because water is colder.
D) Because honey is heavier.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's objection is deflected by al-Razi's definition of moisture as a quality of ease of adhesion and separation, not adhesion alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you settled for a simplistic definition of a complex reality in your own life, only to later realize it was a straw man? How might you embrace al-Razi's call for nuance even when it feels easier to stick with the surface?
?
Ibn Sina's objection targets a definition that is not the refined one. Reflect on a time you argued against a position you didn't fully understand. How can you ensure that your criticisms are aimed at the strongest version of an idea, not the weakest?
?
The passage shows that a quality (moisture) is not the same as one of its effects (adhesion). What is a quality of your character that you have reduced to a single behavior? How might a richer understanding change how you seek to grow?

beginner

Two Definitions of Dryness
Al-Jurjani gives two definitions of dryness corresponding to two definitions of wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of dryness offered by al-Jurjani, which correspond to the two interpretations of wetness.

Core Idea
Dryness is defined as the opposite of wetness in two distinct ways.

Al-Jurjani begins the third investigation by stating that dryness is opposite to wetness by agreement. He then provides two alternative definitions, each paired with a previous interpretation of wetness. These definitions will be critically examined later, but here they are presented as the starting point for discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic sentence that states dryness is opposite to wetness by agreement. Notice how it sets up the investigation.
The phrase 'اتفاقاً' emphasizes that this is uncontroversial.
2
Identify the first definition
Find the first definition: dryness is a quality that makes adhesion and separation difficult. Note that this corresponds to the first interpretation of wetness.
3
Identify the second definition
Find the second definition: dryness is a quality that makes shaping and leaving shape difficult. This corresponds to the second interpretation of wetness.
4
Reflect on the pairing
Consider why al-Jurjani gives two definitions. The passage does not yet judge which is correct; it simply presents them as options.

وثالثها أن اليبوسة تقابل الرطوبة اتفاقاً (فهي إما عسر الالتصاق ) والانفصال أي كيفية تقتضي عسرهما على التفسير الأول للرطوبة . ( أو عسر التشكل وتركه أي كيفية تقتضي ذلك على التفسير الثاني لها . قال الإمام الرازي: لعل الأقرب في بيان حقيقة اليابس أن يقال : ( من الأجسام التي نشاهدها (ما يسهل تفرقه ويصعب اتصاله إما لذاته بأن يكون ذلك الجسم في نفسه بحيث تتفرق أجزاؤه وتنفرك بسهولة ( وهو اليابس ) . فاليبوسة حينئذ هي الكيفية التي يكون الجسم بها سهل التفرق عسر الاجتماع ( وإما اللحامات ( سهلة الانفراك (بين أجزائه) الصغيرة ( الصلبة) التي يكون كل واحد منها عسر التفرق في نفسه (وهو الهش ومنها ما هو بالعكس مما ذكر ( فيسهل اتصاله ويصعب تفرقه وهو اللزج قال : وهذا ما وجدته في مباحث ابن قرة الثابت) هذا الكلام منقول من المباحث المشرقية وليس فيه ذكر اللزج في التقسيم المنسوب إلى الثابت والمذكور في الملخص أن من. قوله : ( لعل الأقرب إلخ) لعل وجه الأقربية أنه قال : أولاً لو فسرنا اليبوسة بالكيفية التي باعتبارها يعسر قبول الأشكال . لم يبق بينها وبين الصلابة فرق . ثم قال بعد نقل ما قاله ابن قرة فظهر الفرق بين اليبس والهشاشة وبين الصلابة. وأنت خبير بوضوح الفرق لأن الصلابة كيفية بها ممانعة الغامز وأين هذا من اليبوسة؟ قوله : ( فاليبوسة حينئذ هي الكيفية إلخ ) على هذا لا تكون اليبوسة من الملموسات ولا يكون الحجر يابساً ويكون النار ،رطباً، لأنه وإن كانت سهلة التفرق لكنها ليست عسيرة الاجتماع أو تكون واسطة ولعل هذه اليبوسة بمعنى الجفاف. فإن الجسم المبتل إذا أثر فيه الرطوبة الغريبة يصعب تفرقه ويسهل اجتماعه عما كان قبله . وإذا جف صار الأمر بالعكس. قوله : ( في التقسيم المنسوب إلخ) لكن ذكره الإمام في فصل بيان الهشاشة واللزوجة . قوله: (والمذكور إلخ) يعني اكتفى في تفسيرها بسهولة الانفراك وعدمها وجعل مقابل الهش واليابس، الصلب ولا يخفى أنه ليس مقابلا لهما . قوله : ( فهي إما عسر الالتصاق والانفصال إلخ) قيل : فعلى هذا يكون بينهما واسطة إذ ما يعسر به واحد منها، ويسهل الآخر فهو لا رطب ولا يابس ولهذا قال الإمام : هذا التعريف بالصلابة أجدر . قوله : ( أو عسر التشكل وتركه يرد على هذا التعريف بأنه صادق على الصلابة الموجودة عند الفلاسفة اللهم إلا أن يثبت استلزام الصلابة لليبوسة وإن ذلك العسر في الجسم الصلب لأجل يبوسته لا لأجل صلابته وأنى ذلك الإثبات ؟
And the third of them: that dryness is opposite to wetness by agreement. (So it is either difficulty of adhesion and separation) i.e., a quality requiring their difficulty according to the first interpretation of wetness, (or difficulty of shaping and leaving shape) i.e., a quality requiring that according to the second interpretation of it. Imam al-Rāzī said: Perhaps the closest in explaining the reality of the dry is to say: (Among the bodies we observe, there is that whose dispersion is easy and whose joining is difficult, either by itself, such that that body in itself is such that its parts separate and crumble easily—that is the dry. So dryness then is the quality by which the body is easy to disperse and difficult to join. (And there are adhesions) easy to crumble between its small, hard parts, each of which is difficult to disperse in itself—that is the brittle. And among them is the opposite of what was mentioned: its joining is easy and its dispersion difficult—that is the sticky. He said: This is what I found in the investigations of Ibn Qurra al-Thābit.) This statement is quoted from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, and there is no mention of the sticky in the division attributed to al-Thābit, and it is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhaṣ that from ... His saying: ('Perhaps the closest etc.') – Perhaps the reason for its being closer is that he said: First, if we defined dryness as the quality by which it is difficult to accept shapes, no difference would remain between it and solidity. Then after transmitting what Ibn Qurra said, the distinction became clear between dryness and brittleness, and between them and solidity. And you are expert in the clarity of the distinction because solidity is a quality by which one resists pressure, and how is that related to dryness? His saying: ('So dryness then is the quality etc.') – According to this, dryness would not be among tangible qualities, nor would stone be dry, and fire would be wet, because although it is easy to disperse, it is not difficult to join, or it would be intermediate. Perhaps this dryness means aridity. For a moistened body, when foreign moisture affects it, its dispersion becomes difficult and its joining easy compared to before; when it dries, the opposite occurs. His saying: ('In the division attributed to etc.') – But the Imam mentioned it in the chapter on explaining brittleness and stickiness. His saying: ('And what is mentioned etc.') – meaning he sufficed, in its interpretation, with ease of crumbling or its absence, and made the opposite of the brittle and the dry to be the solid; it is not hidden that it is not opposite to them. His saying: ('So it is either difficulty of adhesion and separation etc.') – It is said: According to this, there would be an intermediate between them, because that which is difficult in one of them and easy in the other is neither wet nor dry; therefore the Imam said: This definition is more worthy of solidity. His saying: ('Or difficulty of shaping and leaving shape') – This definition is objected to because it applies to the solidity found among the philosophers, unless it is established that solidity necessarily implies dryness, and that difficulty in a solid body is due to its dryness, not its solidity; and how can that be established?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. It presents the third investigation, which states that dryness (yubūsa) is opposite to wetness (ruṭūba) by agreement. Two definitions are given for dryness, corresponding to the two previous definitions of wetness: (1) difficulty of adhesion (iltiṣāq) and separation (infiṣāl), i.e., a quality requiring both to be difficult; (2) difficulty of shaping (tashakkul) and leaving shape. Then Imam al-Rāzī offers an alternative definition: the dry (yābis) is a body whose parts are easy to disperse and hard to join. Dryness is then the quality that makes the body easy to disperse and hard to join. He distinguishes three categories: dry (yābis), brittle (hashsh) – where small hard parts are easy to crumble from each other, and sticky (lazij) – where joining is easy and dispersion difficult. Al-Rāzī attributes this to Ibn Qurra al-Thābit, but notes that the sticky is not mentioned in the division attributed to al-Thābit in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, and al-Mulakhkhaṣ has a different classification. The subsequent marginal notes critique these definitions. The first note (قوله: لعل الأقرب) explains why al-Rāzī's definition is closer: earlier definitions (like difficulty of shaping) blur the line between dryness and solidity (ṣalāba). The note shows that solidity is resistance to pressure, different from dryness. The second note (قوله: فاليبوسة حينئذ) points out a problem: if dryness is ease of dispersion and difficulty of joining, then stone would not be dry (since it is hard to disperse) and fire would be wet (since it is easy to disperse). It suggests this definition might actually refer to aridity (jafāf), giving the example of a moist body that becomes easier to disperse when it dries. The third note (قوله: في التقسيم المنسوب) clarifies that al-Rāzī does mention stickiness elsewhere. The fourth note (قوله: والمذكور) criticizes a different classification that opposes brittle and dry to solid, noting that solid is not a true opposite. The fifth note (قوله: فهي إما عسر) argues that the first definition (difficulty of adhesion and separation) implies an intermediate state where one is difficult and the other easy, which is neither wet nor dry; thus the Imam said this definition is more suited for solidity. The final note (قوله: أو عسر التشكل) objects that the second definition applies equally to philosophical solidity, unless one can prove that solidity entails dryness—a difficult task.

Key Terms
يبوسة (yubūsa)
عسر الالتصاق (ʿusr al-iltiṣāq)
عسر الانفصال (ʿusr al-infiṣāl)
عسر التشكل (ʿusr al-tashakkul)
ترك التشكل (tark al-tashakkul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many definitions of dryness are given by al-Jurjani?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani presents two definitions of dryness, each paired with a different interpretation of wetness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Dryness is defined as difficulty of adhesion and separation. Reflect on a relationship in your life where you have been 'dry'—difficult to connect with or let go of. How has that affected you and others? What would it mean to become 'wet' in that relationship? Write one concrete step you can take today.
?
Dryness is also difficulty of shaping. Think of a habit or pattern in your life that has become 'dry'—rigid and unchangeable. Where do you need to allow yourself to be shaped by Allah's guidance? Identify one area where you resist change and ask yourself why.

What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
Al-Rāzī's Alternative Definition of Dryness
Al-Rāzī presents a new definition of dryness and classifies bodies into dry, brittle, and sticky.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Rāzī's definition of dryness and his three categories: dry, brittle, and sticky.

Core Idea
Dryness is the quality of being easy to disperse and hard to join.

Imam al-Rāzī offers a different approach to defining dryness, focusing on observable behavior of bodies. He introduces three categories based on ease or difficulty of dispersion and joining, providing a more detailed classification than the earlier definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Rāzī's definition
Read the Arabic where al-Rāzī says the dry is a body whose parts are easy to disperse and hard to join. Note that he defines dryness as the quality causing this.
2
Distinguish the three categories
Identify the three types: dry (yābis), brittle (hashsh), and sticky (lazij). Notice the criteria: ease/difficulty of dispersion and joining.
3
Understand the brittle
The brittle has small hard parts that are easy to crumble apart from each other, though each part itself is hard to disperse.
4
Understand the sticky
The sticky is the opposite: easy to join and hard to disperse.

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. It presents the third investigation, which states that dryness (yubūsa) is opposite to wetness (ruṭūba) by agreement. Two definitions are given for dryness, corresponding to the two previous definitions of wetness: (1) difficulty of adhesion (iltiṣāq) and separation (infiṣāl), i.e., a quality requiring both to be difficult; (2) difficulty of shaping (tashakkul) and leaving shape. Then Imam al-Rāzī offers an alternative definition: the dry (yābis) is a body whose parts are easy to disperse and hard to join. Dryness is then the quality that makes the body easy to disperse and hard to join. He distinguishes three categories: dry (yābis), brittle (hashsh) – where small hard parts are easy to crumble from each other, and sticky (lazij) – where joining is easy and dispersion difficult. Al-Rāzī attributes this to Ibn Qurra al-Thābit, but notes that the sticky is not mentioned in the division attributed to al-Thābit in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, and al-Mulakhkhaṣ has a different classification. The subsequent marginal notes critique these definitions. The first note (قوله: لعل الأقرب) explains why al-Rāzī's definition is closer: earlier definitions (like difficulty of shaping) blur the line between dryness and solidity (ṣalāba). The note shows that solidity is resistance to pressure, different from dryness. The second note (قوله: فاليبوسة حينئذ) points out a problem: if dryness is ease of dispersion and difficulty of joining, then stone would not be dry (since it is hard to disperse) and fire would be wet (since it is easy to disperse). It suggests this definition might actually refer to aridity (jafāf), giving the example of a moist body that becomes easier to disperse when it dries. The third note (قوله: في التقسيم المنسوب) clarifies that al-Rāzī does mention stickiness elsewhere. The fourth note (قوله: والمذكور) criticizes a different classification that opposes brittle and dry to solid, noting that solid is not a true opposite. The fifth note (قوله: فهي إما عسر) argues that the first definition (difficulty of adhesion and separation) implies an intermediate state where one is difficult and the other easy, which is neither wet nor dry; thus the Imam said this definition is more suited for solidity. The final note (قوله: أو عسر التشكل) objects that the second definition applies equally to philosophical solidity, unless one can prove that solidity entails dryness—a difficult task.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Critiques of the Definitions of Dryness
Marginal notes critique the definitions, distinguishing dryness from solidity and highlighting logical problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key criticisms of the definitions of dryness, especially the confusion with solidity and the problem of intermediates.

Core Idea
Defining dryness requires careful distinction from solidity and other qualities.

The passage continues with several marginal notes that critique the definitions presented earlier. These notes highlight problems such as the overlap with solidity, the implication of intermediates, and inconsistencies in classification. They show that defining simple qualities like dryness is not straightforward.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of al-Rāzī's definition
Read the note that objects to al-Rāzī's definition because it would make stone not dry and fire wet. Understand the issue.
2
Understand the distinction from solidity
The note explains that solidity is resistance to pressure, while dryness is something else. This addresses a common confusion.
3
Consider the problem of intermediates
The note on 'فهي إما عسر الالتصاق' points out that the first definition implies an intermediate state, which is problematic.
4
Reflect on the second definition
The final note argues that the second definition (difficulty of shaping) also applies to solidity, unless solidity implies dryness—which is hard to prove.
